822 <h1>

823Childhood Vascular Tumors Treatment (PDQ)Health Professional Version</h1>

824

825 <div id="cgvBody">

826

827 <div class="summary-sections">

828

829 <nav class="on-this-page" role="navigation">

830

831 <h6>

832On This Page</h6>

833

834 <ul>

835

836 <li>

837<a href="#_385">

838Vascular Malformations</a>

839</li>

840

841 <li>

842<a href="#_1">

843Childhood Vascular Tumors</a>

844</li>

846 <li>

847<a href="#_193">

848Special Considerations for the Treatment of Children With Cancer</a>

849</li>

889

890 <section id="_385">

891

892 <h2>

893Vascular Malformations</h2>

894

895 <div id="_section_385" class="pdq-sections">

896<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

897<h6>

898In This Section</h6>

899<ul>

900<li>

901<a href="#_2448_toc">

902Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation</a>

903</li>

904</ul>

905</nav>

906<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

907<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/patient/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq">

908Go to Patient Version</a>

909</div>

910<p id="_426" tabindex="-1">

911While information about vascular malformations is covered at the beginning of this summary, the remainder of this summary focuses on tumors, not malformations.</p>

912<p id="_386" tabindex="-1">

913Although not considered tumors, many vascular malformations are caused by targetable somatic mutations. This discovery means that pediatric oncologists will be asked to be involved in management of these lesions. Therefore, it is important for oncologists to have some understanding of the biology and clinical management of common vascular malformations. </p>

914<p id="_475" tabindex="-1">

915Vascular anomalies are a spectrum of rare diseases classified as vascular tumors or malformations. Generally, vascular tumors are proliferative, while malformations enlarge through expansion of a developmental anomaly without underlying proliferation.</p>

916<p id="_387" tabindex="-1">

917Vascular malformations are distinguished from vascular tumors by their low cell turnover and lack of invasiveness.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9181</a>

919] They tend to grow in proportion to the child and are generally stable in adulthood. Nonetheless, endothelial cells isolated from vascular malformations have been found <em>

920in vitro</em>

921 to have some tumor-like behaviors, such as increased growth, migration, and resistance to apoptosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9222</a>

923]</p>

924<p id="_388" tabindex="-1">

925In the International Society for the Study of Vascular Anomalies (ISSVA) classification, vascular malformations are subdivided according to vessel type.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9263</a>

927] Fast-flow lesions include arterial-venous fistulas (AVF) and arterial-venous malformations (AVM). These lesions are very complicated and can cause bleeding, ulceration, and organ dysfunction.</p>

928<p id="_2446" tabindex="-1">

929Slow-flow lesions include venous, lymphatic, capillary, or combined lesions. Complications from slow-flow lesions include pain, infection, bleeding, thrombosis, and organ dysfunction.</p>

930<p id="_2447" tabindex="-1">

931Treatment of patients with vascular malformations requires an interdisciplinary approach to care and includes observation, surgery, endovascular intervention, and medical management. Only a low level of evidence supports the choice of treatment between these options. Recurrence rates of these lesions are relatively high.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

9324</a>

933]</p>

934<p id="_389" tabindex="-1">

935Vascular malformations are most commonly caused by mutations in the MAP2K/PIK3CA pathway. Most are activating somatic mutations but, rarely, germline mutations are identified. Approximately one-third to one-half of venous malformations result from somatic or, rarely, germline mutations in the <em class="gene-name">

936TEK</em>

937 (or <em class="gene-name">

938TIE2</em>

939) gene.[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

9405</a>

941] Another one-third of venous malformations, and nearly all lymphatic malformations, are caused by somatic mutations in <em class="gene-name">

942PIK3CA</em>

943.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

9446</a>

945] In most cases, <em class="gene-name">

946PIK3CA</em>

947 mutations are identical to canonical cancer mutations. Lesions harboring <em class="gene-name">

948PIK3CA</em>

949 mutations are frequently associated with overgrowth of adjacent tissues, as seen in patients with Klippel-Tr&#233;naunay syndrome and CLOVES syndrome (congenital lipomatous overgrowth, vascular malformations, epidermal nevis, spinal/skeletal anomalies/scoliosis).[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

9507</a>

951] </p>

952<p id="_2453" tabindex="-1">

953Sirolimus was initially used to target the PI3K pathway in slow-flow malformations, leading to symptomatic improvement in many patients. It is unclear whether treatment reduces the size of lesions because there is usually considerable fluctuation in size, and treatment generally begins when lesions are enlarged. The use of sirolimus in venous and lymphatic malformations is supported by level C evidence (case series, other observational study designs, phase II studies).[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

9548</a>

955-<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

95610</a>

957] Both <em class="gene-name">

958PIK3CA</em>

959- and <em class="gene-name">

960TEK</em>

961-mutated lesions appear to respond equally to treatment with sirolimus. Phase III clinical trials are underway in Europe (e.g., <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT02638389">

962NCT02638389</a>

963, <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03987152">

964NCT03987152</a>

965, and <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT04980872">

966NCT04980872</a>

967). A 2018 study reported promising level C evidence for the use of the PI3K inhibitor BYL719 (alpelisib) to treat patients who have lesions with a <em class="gene-name">

968PIK3CA</em>

969 mutation.[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

97011</a>

971] From these studies, preliminary FDA approval has been obtained. For information about ongoing studies, see the <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_2448">

972Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation</a>

973 section.</p>

974<p id="_390" tabindex="-1">

975There is some support for targeted therapy in fast-flow malformations and complicated lymphatic anomalies that are caused by somatic and germline mutations in the MAPK pathway, including gain of function mutations in <em class="gene-name">

976MAP2K1</em>

977, <em class="gene-name">

978KRAS</em>

979, <em class="gene-name">

980NRAS</em>

981, and <em class="gene-name">

982BRAF</em>

983.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

98412</a>

985] Limited data suggest that MEK pathway inhibition may soon have a role in treating patients with these aggressive, highly symptomatic, and sometimes fatal lesions.[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

98613</a>

987-<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

98820</a>

989] For information about ongoing studies, see the <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_2448">

990Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation</a>

991 section.</p>

992<section id="_2448">

993<h3 id="_2448_toc">

994Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation</h3>

995<p id="_2449" tabindex="-1">

996Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)-supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

997NCI website</a>

998. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

999ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

1000.</p>

1001<p id="_2450" tabindex="-1">

1002The following are examples of national and/or institutional clinical trials that are currently being conducted:</p>

1003<div class="pdq-content-list">

1004<ul id="_2451">

1005<li>

1006<strong>

1007<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT04589650">

1008NCT04589650</a>

1009</strong>

1010 (Study Assessing the Efficacy, Safety, and Pharmacokinetics [PK] of Alpelisib [BYL719] in Pediatric and Adult Patients With <em class="gene-name">

1011PIK3CA</em>

1012-Related Overgrowth Spectrum [EPIK-P2])<strong>

1013:</strong>

1014 This study consists of a screening period, core period, extension period, and long-term extension period to assess the efficacy and PK of alpelisib.</li>

1015<li>

1016<strong>

1017<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT04258046">

1018NCT04258046</a>

1019</strong>

1020 (Trametinib in the Treatment of Complicated Extracranial Arterial Venous Malformation)<strong>

1021:</strong>

1022 This is a phase II study to assess the safety and efficacy of trametinib in the treatment of children and adults.</li>

1023<li>

1024<strong>

1025<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT05125471">

1026NCT05125471</a>

1027</strong>

1028 (Cobimetinib in Extracranial Arteriovenous Malformations [COBI-AVM Study])<strong>

1029:</strong>

1030 This is a phase II study to evaluate the safety and efficacy of cobimetinib in the treatment of children and adults.</li>

1031</ul>

1032</div>

1033</section>

1034<h6 do-not-show="toc">

1035References</h6>

1036<ol>

Plast Reconstr Surg 69 (3): 412-22, 1982.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=7063565&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=7063565&amp;dopt=Abstract">

1039[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

1040</li>

1124 <section id="_1">

1125

1126 <h2>

1127Childhood Vascular Tumors</h2>

1128

1129 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

1130<p id="_301" tabindex="-1">

1131Vascular tumors are proliferative tumors that can be benign or malignant. Growth and/or expansion of vascular tumors can cause clinical problems such as disfigurement, chronic pain, coagulopathies, organ dysfunction, and death.</p>

1132<p id="_303" tabindex="-1">

1133The quality of evidence regarding childhood vascular tumors is limited by retrospective data collection, small sample size, cohort selection and participation bias, and heterogeneity of the disorders.</p>

1134<p id="_302" tabindex="-1">

1135In the past, limited treatment options were available and efficacy was not validated in prospective clinical trials. Historically, therapies consisted of interventional and surgical procedures used to palliate symptoms. Limited medical therapies were available. Newer therapy options with propranolol and sirolimus are now available for the treatment of patients with complex vascular tumors. The first prospective clinical trial using propranolol for infantile hemangioma has been published, as well as the first prospective clinical trial that studied the effectiveness of sirolimus for complicated vascular anomalies, including vascular tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

11361</a>

1137,<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

11382</a>

1139]</p>

1140<p id="_8" tabindex="-1">

1141With a prevalence of 4% to 5%, infantile hemangiomas are the most common benign tumors of infancy. Other vascular tumors are rare. The classification of these tumors has been difficult, especially in the pediatric population, because of their rarity, unusual morphologic appearance, diverse clinical behavior, and the lack of independent stratification for pediatric tumors. In 2020, the World Health Organization (WHO) updated the classification of soft tissue vascular tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

11423</a>

1143] </p>

1144<p id="_476" tabindex="-1">

1145The International Society for the Study of Vascular Anomalies (ISSVA) classification of tumors is based on the WHO classification but the ISSVA classification uses more precise terminology and phenotypes that have been agreed upon by the members of ISSVA. An updated classification system was adopted at the General Assembly of the ISSVA in April of 2014, and further additions were added in 2018 (<a href="https://www.issva.org/classification" title="https://www.issva.org/classification">

1146ISSVA</a>

1147, May 2018).[<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

11484</a>

1149,<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

11505</a>

1151] For more information, see Tables <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_125">

11521</a>

1153 and <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_142">

11542</a>

1155.</p>

1487International Society for the Study of Vascular Anomalies: ISSVA Classification of Vascular Anomalies. Milwaukee, Wi: International Society for the Study of Vascular Anomalies, 2018. <a href="https://www.issva.org/UserFiles/file/ISSVA-Classification-2018.pdf" title="https://www.issva.org/UserFiles/file/ISSVA-Classification-2018.pdf">

1488Available online</a>

1489. Last accessed June 7, 2022.</li>

1490<li id="section_2.5">

1491Wassef M, Blei F, Adams D, et al.: Vascular Anomalies Classification: Recommendations From the International Society for the Study of Vascular Anomalies. Pediatrics 136 (1): e203-14, 2015.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=26055853&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=26055853&amp;dopt=Abstract">

1492[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

1493</li>

1494</ol>

1495</div>

1496

1497 </section>

1498

1499

1500

1501 <section id="_193">

1502

1503 <h2>

1504Special Considerations for the Treatment of Children With Cancer</h2>

1505

1506 <div id="_section_193" class="pdq-sections">

1507<p id="_194" tabindex="-1">

1508Cancer in children and adolescents is rare, although the overall incidence has been slowly increasing since 1975.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

15091</a>

1510] Children and adolescents with

1511cancer should be referred to medical centers that have a multidisciplinary team

1512of cancer specialists with experience treating the cancers that occur during

1513childhood and adolescence. This multidisciplinary team approach incorporates the skills

1514of the following health care professionals and

1515others to ensure that children receive treatment, supportive care, and

1516rehabilitation that will achieve optimal survival and quality of life:</p>

1517<div class="pdq-content-list">

1518<ul id="_2391">

1519<li>

1520Primary care physicians.</li>

1521<li>

1522Surgeons experienced in vascular

1523tumors.</li>

1524<li>

1525Pathologists.</li>

1526<li>

1527Radiation oncologists.</li>

1528<li>

1529Pediatric oncologists.</li>

1530<li>

1531Rehabilitation specialists.</li>

1532<li>

1533Pediatric nurse specialists.</li>

1534<li>

1535Social workers.</li>

1536</ul>

1537</div>

1538<p id="_2392" tabindex="-1">

1539

1540For specific information about supportive care for children and adolescents with cancer, see the summaries on <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/information-summaries/supportive-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/information-summaries/supportive-care">

1541Supportive and Palliative Care</a>

1542.</p>

1543<p id="_195" tabindex="-1">

1544The American Academy of

1545Pediatrics has outlined guidelines for pediatric cancer centers and their role in the treatment of

1546pediatric patients with cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

15472</a>

1548] At these pediatric cancer centers, clinical trials are

1549available for most types of cancer that occur in children and

1550adolescents, and the opportunity to participate is offered to

1551most patients and families. Clinical trials for children and adolescents with

1552cancer are generally designed to compare potentially better therapy with

1553current standard therapy. Most of the progress

1554made in identifying curative therapies for childhood cancers has been achieved

1555through clinical trials. Information about ongoing clinical trials is

1556available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

1557NCI website</a>

1558.</p>

1559<p id="_196" tabindex="-1">

1560Dramatic improvements in survival have been achieved for children and adolescents with cancer. Between 1975 and 2020, childhood cancer mortality decreased by more than 50%.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

15611</a>

1562,<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

15633</a>

1564,<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

15654</a>

1566] Childhood and adolescent cancer survivors require close monitoring because side effects of cancer therapy may persist or develop months or years after treatment. For specific information about the incidence, type, and monitoring of late effects in childhood and adolescent cancer survivors, see <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/late-effects-hp-pdq">

1567Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer</a>

1568.</p>

1569<h6 do-not-show="toc">

1570References</h6>

1571<ol>

Cancer 120 (16): 2497-506, 2014.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=24853691&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=24853691&amp;dopt=Abstract">

1574[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

1575</li>

1578Also available online</a>

1579. Last accessed May 19, 2023.</li>

1783Juvenile nasopharyngeal angiofibroma is not included in the World Health Organization classification or the International Society for the Study of Vascular Anomalies classification of vascular tumors. It is included here because growing evidence reveals vascular differentiation and proliferation in these tumors with response to vascular remodeling and antiproliferative agents. </p>

1784</li>

1786</div>

1787<section id="_9">

1788<h3 id="_9_toc">

1789Infantile Hemangioma</h3>

1790<section id="_10">

1791<h4 id="_10_toc">

1792Incidence and epidemiology</h4>

1793<p id="_11" tabindex="-1">

1794Infantile hemangiomas (IH) are the most common benign vascular tumor of infancy, occurring in 4% to 5% of infants. The true incidence is unknown.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

17951</a>

1796] They are not usually present at birth and are diagnosed most commonly at age 3 to 6 weeks.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

17972</a>

1798-<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

17995</a>

1800] The lesion proliferates for an average of 5 months, stabilizes, and then involutes over several years.</p>

1801<p id="_304" tabindex="-1">

1802Infantile hemangiomas are more common in females, non-Hispanic White patients, and premature infants. Multiple hemangiomas are more common in infants who are the product of multiple gestations or <em>

1803in vitro</em>

1804 fertilization.[<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

18055</a>

1806-<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

18077</a>

1808] Infantile hemangiomas are associated with advanced maternal age, placenta previa, pre-eclampsia, and other placental anomalies.[<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

18095</a>

1810] </p>

1811</section>

1812<section id="_12">

1813<h4 id="_12_toc">

1814Clinical presentation</h4>

1815<p id="_13" tabindex="-1">

1816Most infantile hemangiomas are not present at birth but precursor lesions such as telangiectasia or faint discoloration of the skin or hypopigmentation can often be seen. The lesion can be mistaken as a bruise from birth trauma or as a capillary malformation (port-wine stain) (see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_245">

1817Figure 1</a>

1818).[<a href="#cit/section_4.8">

18198</a>

1820,<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

18219</a>

1822]</p>

1823<p id="_244" tabindex="-1">

1824<figure id="figure_245" class="image-center">

1825<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/787423.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1826Enlarge</a>

1827<img id="_245" alt="Photos showing an infantile hemangioma premonitory mark; the photos on the left show a precursor lesion (faint color with halo). The photos on the right show a hemangioma after proliferation (slightly raised with a brighter central color)." title="Photos showing an infantile hemangioma premonitory mark; the photos on the left show a precursor lesion (faint color with halo). The photos on the right show a hemangioma after proliferation (slightly raised with a brighter central color)." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/787423-750.jpg">

1828<figcaption class="caption-container">

1829Figure 1. The photos on the left depict the precursor lesion (faint color with halo). The photos on the right depict the hemangioma after proliferation (slightly raised with a brighter central color). Credit: Israel Fernandez-Pineda, M.D.</figcaption>

1830</figure>

1831</p>

1832<p id="_243" tabindex="-1">

1833Infantile hemangiomas can be superficial in the dermis, deep in the subcutaneous tissue, combined, or in the viscera. Combined lesions are common and generally appear in the head and neck but can be anywhere on the body. </p>

1834<p id="_398" tabindex="-1">

1835Infantile hemangiomas can be characterized as follows:</p>

1836<div class="pdq-content-list">

1837<ul id="_399">

1838<li>

1839<strong>

1840Local:</strong>

1841 Most lesions are localized and noted to be in a well-defined area without evidence of a geometric pattern.</li>

1842<li>

1843<strong>

1844Segmental:</strong>

1845 Most segmental hemangiomas occur in the head and neck region (<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_506">

1846PHACE syndrome</a>

1847) but can be seen in the genitourinary area, arm, chest, or legs <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_521">

1848(PELVIS/LUMBAR/SACRAL syndrome</a>

1849). <div class="pdq-content-list">

1850<ul id="_430">

1851<li>

1852Diffuse hemangiomas of the face demonstrate defined cutaneous patterns. Several studies have evaluated the distributions of these hemangiomas and found the following four distinct patterns or segments: <div class="pdq-content-list">

1853<ul id="_2438">

1854<li>

1855Segment 1 involves the lateral forehead, anterior temporal scalp, and the lateral frontal scalp. </li>

1856<li>

1857Segments 2 and 3 are located over the maxillary and mandibular area.</li>

1858<li>

1859Segment 4 covers the medial frontal scalp, nose, and philtrum.</li>

1860</ul>

1861</div>

1862</li>

1863</ul>

1864</div>

1865<p id="_431" tabindex="-1">

1866Two papers have noted this observation and suggest the involvement of neural crest derivatives in facial hemangioma development.[<a href="#cit/section_4.10">

186710</a>

1868,<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

186911</a>

1870] Segmental hemangiomas commonly occur in females and are more likely associated with complications and other syndromes.[<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

187112</a>

1872,<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

187313</a>

1874]</p>

1875 <p id="_2454" tabindex="-1">

1876 For information about PHACE syndrome or PELVIS/LUMBAR/SACRAL syndrome, see the <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_16">

1877Syndromes associated with infantile hemangioma</a>

1878 section.</p>

1879</li>

1880<li>

1881<strong>

1882Multiple:</strong>

1883 More than one lesion, but noted in the past as greater than five lesions, because of the increased risk of visceral involvement (mostly the liver). </li>

1884</ul>

1885</div>

1886<p id="_290" tabindex="-1">

1887The cutaneous appearance of infantile hemangiomas is usually red to crimson, firm, and warm in the proliferative phase. The lesion then lightens centrally and becomes less warm and softer; it then flattens and loses its color. The process of involution can take several years and once involution has occurred, regrowth is uncommon. In two patients treated with growth hormone, regrowth after involution was noted.[<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

188814</a>

1889] On further investigation, growth hormone receptors were found on the infantile hemangioma cells. Although preliminary, this may advance the research into the etiology of hemangioma growth.</p>

1890<p id="_329" tabindex="-1">

1891Permanent sequelae, such as telangiectasia, anetodermal skin, redundant skin, and a persistent superficial component, can occur after hemangioma involution (see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_341">

1892Figure 2</a>

1893). Rare instances of dysesthesias in sites of involuted infantile hemangiomas in the absence of ulceration have been described.[<a href="#cit/section_4.15">

189415</a>

1895][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1896Level of evidence C1</a>

1897] In a retrospective cohort study of 184 hemangiomas, the overall incidence of significant sequelae was 54.9%. Sequelae were more common in combined hemangiomas, hemangiomas with a step or abrupt border, and cobblestone surface hemangiomas. Furthermore, this study revealed that the average age to hemangioma involution was 3.5 years.[<a href="#cit/section_4.16">

189816</a>

1899]</p>

1900<p id="_340" tabindex="-1">

1901<figure id="figure_341" class="image-center">

1902<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/791441.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1903Enlarge</a>

1904<img id="_341" alt="Photographs showing different types of hemangioma sequelae, pre-progression and post-progression." title="Photographs showing different types of hemangioma sequelae, pre-progression and post-progression." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/791441-750.jpg">

1905<figcaption class="caption-container">

1906Figure 2. Examples of different types of sequelae. A, deep hemangioma that regressed without sequelae; B, superficial hemangioma that left only telangiectasia; C, mixed hemangioma that left anetodermic skin; D, mixed hemangioma that left redundant skin; and E, mixed hemangioma that left fibrofatty tissue. Reproduced with permission from JAMA Dermatology. 2016. 152 (11): 1239&#8211;1243. Copyright &#169; (2016) American Medical Association. All rights reserved.</figcaption>

1907</figure>

1908</p>

1909</section>

1910<section id="_34">

1911<h4 id="_34_toc">

1912Biology and histopathology</h4>

1913<p id="_35" tabindex="-1">

1914Most infantile hemangiomas occur sporadically. However, they may rarely be caused by an abnormality of chromosome 5 and present in an autosomal dominant pattern.[<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

191517</a>

1916] In a study that evaluated inheritance patterns of infantile hemangiomas, 34% of patients had a family history of infantile hemangioma, most commonly in a first-degree relative.[<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

191717</a>

1918,<a href="#cit/section_4.18">

191918</a>

1920]</p>

1921<p id="_394" tabindex="-1">

1922The exact mechanism that causes the initial proliferation of blood vessels followed by involution of the vascular component of hemangioma and replacement of fibrofatty tissue is unknown. Several cell types have been isolated from hemangiomas: progenitor/stem cells (HemSC), endothelial cells (HemEC), pericytes (HemPericytes), and mast cells.[<a href="#cit/section_4.19">

192319</a>

1924,<a href="#cit/section_4.20">

192520</a>

1926] These cells appear to play a role in the development of infantile hemangiomas.</p>

1927<p id="_433" tabindex="-1">

1928HemSC represent a small percentage of proliferating hemangioma cells and have the ability for self renewal and multilineage differentiation. These cells differentiate into endothelial cells, adipocytes, and pericytes. When HemSC are implanted into immunodeficient mice, hemangioma-like lesions form and then spontaneously regress, similar to infantile hemangiomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

192921</a>

1930] This suggests that infantile hemangioma proliferation occurs during vasculogenesis (the formation of new blood vessels from angioblasts), as opposed to angiogenesis (the formation of new blood vessels from existing blood vessels).</p>

1931<p id="_422" tabindex="-1">

1932HemEC are plump, metabolically active, and resemble fetal endothelial cells in the proliferative phase. Evaluation of infantile hemangioma endothelial cells suggest that they are clonal in nature.[<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

193321</a>

1934-<a href="#cit/section_4.23">

193523</a>

1936]</p>

1937<p id="_395" tabindex="-1">

1938HemPericytes surround the vasculature and are abundant in the proliferative phase. These cells express markers of pericytes and smooth muscle cells, such as neural-glial antigen 2 (NG2), platelet-derived growth factor receptor beta (PDGFR-beta), calponin, alpha smooth muscle actin (SMA), and NOTCH3. HemPericytes are proangiogenic, as they express increased vascular endothelial growth factor A (VEGF-A), decreased angiopoietin-1 (ANGPT1), increased proliferation, increased vessel formation <em>

1939in vivo</em>

1940, and decreased ability to suppress proliferation.[<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

194124</a>

1942] One study reported that proliferating infantile hemangiomas contained higher levels of messenger RNA, proteins for NOTCH1, 3, and 4 receptors and their ligands, and the downstream coactivator <em class="gene-name">

1943MAML1</em>

1944 than did normal skin, involuting infantile hemangiomas, and propranolol-treated infantile hemangiomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.25">

194525</a>

1946]</p>

1947<p id="_396" tabindex="-1">

1948Mast cells are found largely in the early involuting phase, but they are also found in small numbers in the proliferative phase and at the end of involution. Their function in infantile hemangiomas is unknown but they have been shown to play a role in other skin tumors such as basal cell carcinoma, squamous cell carcinoma, and melanoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.20">

194920</a>

1950]</p>

1951<p id="_397" tabindex="-1">

1952Provasculogenic factors are expressed during proliferation; these factors include VEGF, fibroblast growth factor (FGF), CD34, CD31, CD133, lymphatic vessel endothelial hyaluronan receptor 1 (LYVE1), and insulin-like growth factor 2 (IGF-2).[<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

195326</a>

1954-<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

195529</a>

1956] During involution, infantile hemangiomas show increased apoptosis.[<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

195729</a>

1958] During this phase, there are also increased mast cells and levels of metalloproteinase, as well as upregulation of interferon and decreased basic FGF (bFGF).[<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

195929</a>

1960-<a href="#cit/section_4.31">

196131</a>

1962] Throughout proliferation and involution, endothelial cells in infantile hemangioma express a particular phenotype showing positive staining for GLUT1 and placenta-associated antigens (Fc-gamma receptor II, merosin, and Lewis Y antigen). These markers are absent in normal capillaries and in other vascular tumors such as congenital hemangioma and vascular malformations. Placental chorionic villi share these same markers. However, no relationship between hemangiomas and placental chorionic villi has been found.[<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

196326</a>

1964]</p>

1965<p id="_393" tabindex="-1">

1966Hypoxia appears to have a critical role in the pathogenesis of hemangiomas. There is an association between hemangiomas and placental hypoxia, which is increased in prematurity, multiple pregnancies, and placental anomalies.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

19672</a>

1968,<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

19695</a>

1970] Multiple targets of hypoxia [<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

197132</a>

1972,<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

197333</a>

1974] are demonstrated in proliferating hemangiomas, including VEGF-A, GLUT1, and IGF-2.[<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

197526</a>

1976,<a href="#cit/section_4.28">

197728</a>

1978,<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

197934</a>

1980] The hypothesis suggests that a proliferating hemangioma is an attempt to normalize hypoxic tissue that occurred <em>

1981in utero</em>

1982.</p>

1983</section>

1984<section id="_14">

1985<h4 id="_14_toc">

1986Diagnostic evaluation</h4>

1987<p id="_15" tabindex="-1">

1988Infantile hemangiomas are usually diagnosed by the history and clinical appearance. Biopsy is rarely needed and performed only if there is an atypical appearance and/or atypical history and presentation. Imaging is not usually necessary, but diagnostic ultrasonography is beneficial if there is a deeper lesion without a cutaneous component and reveals a well-circumscribed, hypoechoic, high-flow lesion with a typical Doppler wave characteristic.[<a href="#cit/section_4.35">

198935</a>

1990] Additionally, infants with five or more cutaneous hemangiomas should undergo ultrasonography of the liver to screen for hepatic hemangioma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.36">

199136</a>

1992]</p>

1993<section id="_344">

1994<h5 id="_344_toc">

1995Infantile hemangioma with minimal or arrested growth</h5>

1996<p id="_345" tabindex="-1">

1997Infantile hemangioma with minimal or arrested growth (IH-MAG) is a variant of hemangioma that can be confused with capillary malformation because of their unusual characteristics. These hemangiomas are mostly fully formed at birth and are characterized by telangiectasia and venules with light and dark areas of skin coloration (see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_347">

1998Figure 3</a>

1999). They resolve spontaneously and are pathologically GLUT1 positive.[<a href="#cit/section_4.37">

200037</a>

2001] They are mainly located on the lower body but can be present in the head and neck area. If they are segmental, they can be associated with <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_506">

2002PHACE syndrome</a>

2003.[<a href="#cit/section_4.38">

200438</a>

2005] Associated soft tissue hypertrophy may persist through childhood.[<a href="#cit/section_4.39">

200639</a>

2007]</p>

2008<p id="_346" tabindex="-1">

2009<figure id="figure_347" class="image-center">

2010<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/791733.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

2011Enlarge</a>

2012<img id="_347" alt="Photographs showing (A) presentation and (B) resolution of an infantile hemangioma in patient 4 (upper left and right photos), and (C) presentation and (D) resolution of an infantile hemangioma in patient 5 (bottom left and right photos)." title="Photographs showing (A) presentation and (B) resolution of an infantile hemangioma in patient 4 (upper left and right photos), and (C) presentation and (D) resolution of an infantile hemangioma in patient 5 (bottom left and right photos)." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/791733-750.jpg">

2013<figcaption class="caption-container">

2014Figure 3. Patient 4 at (A) presentation and (B) resolution. Patient 5 at (C) presentation and (D) resolution. Ma, E. H., Robertson, S. J., Chow, C. W., and Bekhor, P. S. (2017), Infantile Hemangioma with Minimal or Arrested Growth: Further Observations on Clinical and Histopathologic Findings of this Unique but Underrecognized Entity. Pediatr Dermatol, 34: 64&#8211;71. doi:10.1111/pde.13022. Used with permission.</figcaption>

2015</figure>

2016</p>

2017</section>

2018<section id="_291">

2019<h5 id="_291_toc">

2020Airway infantile hemangioma</h5>

2021<p id="_292" tabindex="-1">

2022Airway infantile hemangiomas are usually associated with segmental hemangiomas in a bearded distribution, which may include all or some of the following&#8212;the preauricular skin, mandible, lower lip, chin, or anterior neck. It is important for an otolaryngologist to proactively assess lesions in this distribution before signs of stridor occur. Airway infantile hemangioma incidence increases with a larger area of bearded involvement.[<a href="#cit/section_4.40">

202340</a>

2024]</p>

2025<p id="_434" tabindex="-1">

2026 Airway infantile hemangiomas can occur without skin lesions. A retrospective study of the Vascular Anomaly Database at the Children's Hospital of Pittsburgh analyzed 761 cases of infantile hemangioma. Thirteen patients (1.7%) had subglottic hemangiomas. Of those 13 patients, 4 (30%) had bearded distributions, 2 (15%) had cutaneous hemangiomas, and 7 (55%) had no cutaneous lesions.[<a href="#cit/section_4.41">

202741</a>

2028] For information about the treatment of airway infantile hemangiomas, see the <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_128">

2029Propranolol therapy</a>

2030 section.</p>

2031</section>

2032<section id="_293">

2033<h5 id="_293_toc">

2034Ophthalmologic involvement of hemangiomas</h5>

2035<p id="_294" tabindex="-1">

2036Periorbital hemangiomas can cause visual compromise.[<a href="#cit/section_4.42">

203742</a>

2038] This usually occurs with hemangiomas of the upper medial eyelid but any hemangioma around the eye that is large enough can distort the cornea or obstruct the visual axis. Subcutaneous periocular hemangiomas can extend into the orbit, causing exophthalmos or globe displacement with only limited cutaneous manifestations. Issues with these lesions include astigmatism from direct pressure of the growing hemangioma, ptosis, proptosis, and strabismus. One of the leading causes of preventable blindness in children is stimulus-deprivation amblyopia caused by hemangioma obstruction. All periorbital hemangiomas or those with any possibility of potential visual impairment should have an ophthalmologic evaluation.</p>

2039<p id="_2393" tabindex="-1">

2040Two institutions in France and Canada performed a retrospective analysis of patients in a vascular anomalies practice. The investigators reviewed the records of all patients with a diagnosis of segmental facial or periorbital focal infantile hemangioma who had clinical photographs and brain magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) available.[<a href="#cit/section_4.43">

204143</a>

2042][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2043Level of evidence C1</a>

2044] The study included 122 children (90 girls, 32 boys; mean age, 16.6 months). Forty-five children (36.9%) had a facial infantile hemangioma larger than 5 cm. Twenty-two patients (18.0%) had PHACES or possible PHACES syndrome. Cerebrovascular structural anomalies were seen in 14 of 22 patients with PHACES syndrome and no patients without PHACES syndrome. Brain anomalies were seen in 6 of 22 patients with PHACES syndrome and 1 patient without PHACES syndrome (<em>

2045P</em>

2046 &lt; .001). Cardiovascular anomalies were seen in six patients, and ocular anomalies were seen in eight patients. Of these 14 patients, 13 had PHACES syndrome. The authors concluded that clinical concern about associated extracutaneous anomalies is warranted for all children with facial segmental or periorbital focal infantile hemangiomas, including those with small hemangiomas.</p>

2047<p id="_350" tabindex="-1">

2048Infantile hemangiomas can occur in the conjunctiva (see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_356">

2049Figure 4</a>

2050). These hemangiomas can be associated with other ophthalmologic abnormalities and are treated with oral or topical beta-blockers.[<a href="#cit/section_4.44">

205144</a>

2052]</p>

2053<figure id="figure_356" class="image-center">

2054<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/795553.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

2055Enlarge</a>

2056<img id="_356" alt="Photographs showing different types of infantile hemangiomas involving the conjunctiva." title="Photographs showing different types of infantile hemangiomas involving the conjunctiva." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/795553-750.jpg">

2057<figcaption class="caption-container">

2058Figure 4. Proposed classification of infantile hemangiomas involving the conjunctiva. Theiler M, Baselga E, Gerth-Kahlert C, et al. Infantile hemangiomas with conjunctival involvement: An underreported occurrence. <em>

2059Pediatr Dermatol.</em>

2060 2017;34:681&#8211;685. <a href="https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/pde.13305" title="https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/pde.13305">

2061https://doi.org/10.1111/pde.13305</a>

2062 Copyright &#169; 2017 John Wiley &amp; Sons, Inc.</figcaption>

2063</figure>

2064</section>

2065</section>

2066<section id="_16">

2067<h4 id="_16_toc">

2068Syndromes associated with infantile hemangioma</h4>

2069<section id="_506">

2070<h5 id="_506_toc">

2071PHACE syndrome</h5>

2072<p id="_507" tabindex="-1">

2073<strong>

2074Posterior fossa&#8211;brain malformations; Hemangiomas; Arterial, Cardiac, and Eye abnormalities (PHACE) syndrome:</strong>

2075 <a href="http://omim.org/entry/606519" title="http://omim.org/entry/606519">

2076PHACE syndrome</a>

2077 represents a spectrum of diseases and is defined by the presence of large segmental infantile hemangiomas, usually on the face or head, but can include the neck, chest, or arm, in association with one or more congenital malformations (see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_520">

2078Figure 5</a>

2079).[<a href="#cit/section_4.45">

208045</a>

2081] PHACE syndrome is more common in girls and in full-term, normal birth weight and singleton infants.[<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

208213</a>

2083,<a href="#cit/section_4.46">

208446</a>

2085-<a href="#cit/section_4.50">

208650</a>

2087] The syndrome is not rare among patients with infantile hemangiomas. A prospective study of 108 infants with large facial hemangiomas observed that 31% of patients had PHACE syndrome.[<a href="#cit/section_4.51">

208851</a>

2089] Rare cases of PHACE syndrome have been reported in infants with hemangiomas smaller than 5 cm.[<a href="#cit/section_4.43">

209043</a>

2091][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2092Level of evidence C1</a>

2093]</p>

2094<p id="_508" tabindex="-1">

2095<figure id="figure_520" class="image-center-medium">

2096<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/779667.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

2097Enlarge</a>

2098<img id="_520" alt="Photograph showing a large segmental hemangioma (plaque-like) in a bearded distribution on the right side of the face." title="Photograph showing a large segmental hemangioma (plaque-like) in a bearded distribution on the right side of the face." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/779667-750.jpg">

2099<figcaption class="caption-container">

2100Figure 5. A large segmental infantile hemangioma (plaque-like) in a bearded distribution. This patient has an increased risk of PHACE syndrome, airway infantile hemangioma, and ulceration. A tracheostomy was placed secondary to a very diffuse airway hemangioma. Credit: Denise Adams, M.D. Garzon MC, Epstein LG, Heyer GL, et al.: PHACE Syndrome: Consensus-Derived Diagnosis and Care Recommendations. J Pediatr 178: 24-33.e2, 2016. PMID: 27659028</figcaption>

2101</figure>

2102</p>

2103<p id="_509" tabindex="-1">

2104Consensus criteria for definite and possible PHACE syndrome were updated at an expert panel meeting, as follows:[<a href="#cit/section_4.45">

210545</a>

2106]</p>

2107<p id="_2455" tabindex="-1">

2108<strong>

2109PHACE</strong>

2110</p>

2111<div class="pdq-content-list">

2112<ul id="_510" class="list-dash">

2113<li>

2114<strong>

2115Posterior fossa abnormalities.</strong>

2116 Posterior fossa malformations include Dandy-Walker complex, cerebellar hypoplasia, atrophy, and dysgenesis/agenesis of the vermis. Effects of these anomalies include developmental delays and pituitary dysfunction.[<a href="#cit/section_4.52">

211752</a>

2118]</li>

2119<li>

2120<strong>

2121Hemangiomas.</strong>

2122[<a href="#cit/section_4.51">

212351</a>

2124,<a href="#cit/section_4.53">

212553</a>

2126-<a href="#cit/section_4.56">

212756</a>

2128] <div class="pdq-content-list">

2129<ul id="_511">

2130<li>

2131A large segmental hemangioma over the face and/or scalp with a surface area of 22 cm<span class="sup">

21322</span>

2133 or greater (5 cm &#215; 4.5 cm).</li>

2134<li>

2135A large segmental hemangioma of the neck, trunk, or proximal upper extremity.</li>

2136</ul>

2137</div>

2138<p id="_512" tabindex="-1">

2139Infants with two major criteria of PHACE (e.g., supraumbilical raphe and coarctation of the aorta) but <strong>

2140lacking</strong>

2141 cutaneous infantile hemangiomas should undergo complete evaluation for PHACE.</p>

2142</li>

2143<li>

2144<strong>

2145Arterial abnormalities.</strong>

2146 Cerebrovascular anomalies can include carotid artery abnormalities (including tortuosity) and absence, dilation/aneurysm, or narrowing of cerebral vessels. These anomalies, especially related to the carotid arteries, can lead to progressive arterial occlusion and even stroke. The risk categories are as follows:[<a href="#cit/section_4.49">

214749</a>

2148,<a href="#cit/section_4.50">

214950</a>

2150,<a href="#cit/section_4.57">

215157</a>

2152-<a href="#cit/section_4.59">

215359</a>

2154]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2155<ul id="_513">

2156<li>

2157<strong>

2158Low risk:</strong>

2159 This category includes patients with arterial anomalies frequently seen in a general screening population. It also includes findings that have either no or very minimal clinical impact on patient outcome, even if rarely seen in the general population. Examples are persistent embryonic arteries, anomalous arterial origin or course, and circle of Willis variants.</li>

2160<li>

2161<strong>

2162Intermediate risk:</strong>

2163 Includes patients with nonstenotic dysgenesis, including those with ectatic or segmentally enlarged arteries. It also includes patients with a narrowing or occlusion of arteries proximal to the circle of Willis, with no perceived hemodynamic risk. An evaluation of the patency of the circle of Willis is essential.</li>

2164<li>

2165<strong>

2166High risk:</strong>

2167 This category includes patients with one or more of the following:<div class="pdq-content-list">

2168<ul id="_514" class="list-dash">

2169<li>

2170Significant narrowing (&gt;25%) or occlusion of principal cerebral vessels within or above the circle of Willis that results in an isolated circulation. </li>

2171<li>

2172Tandem or multiple arterial stenoses associated with complex blood flow that may potentially result in diminished cerebral perfusion. Patients with cerebrovascular stenosis in the setting of coarctation of the aorta are likely at higher risk of transient and permanent neurological ischemic events.</li>

2173<li>

2174Imaging findings in the brain parenchyma suggestive of chronic or silent ischemia, or progressive steno-occlusive disease. These parenchymal brain MRI findings include existing infarction, chronic or border zone ischemic changes, and presence of lenticulostriate collateral dilation or pial collaterals.</li>

2175</ul>

2176</div>

2177</li>

2178</ul>

2179</div>

2180</li>

2181<li>

2182<strong>

2183Cardiac abnormalities.</strong>

2184<div class="pdq-content-list">

2185<ul id="_515">

2186<li>

2187Aortic arch anomalies observed in PHACE syndrome are unusually complex, with involvement of the transverse and descending aorta arch. The arch obstruction is most often long-segment. The obstruction is frequently characterized by areas of arch narrowing with adjacent segments of marked aneurysmal dilatation. </li>

2188</ul>

2189</div>

2190</li>

2191<li>

2192<strong>

2193Eye abnormalities.</strong>

2194

2195Ophthalmologic anomalies can include microphthalmos, retinal vascular abnormalities, persistent fetal retinal vessels, exophthalmos, coloboma, and optic nerve atrophy. These abnormalities are rare and occur in 7% to 10% of patients.[<a href="#cit/section_4.60">

219660</a>

2197] </li>

2198</ul>

2199</div>

2200<p id="_516" tabindex="-1">

2201A retrospective review identified midline rhabdomyomatous mesenchymal hamartomas and chin hamartomas in a small number of children with PHACE or <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_521">

2202LUMBAR syndrome</a>

2203.[<a href="#cit/section_4.61">

220461</a>

2205] These are not currently included as minor criteria.</p>

2206<p id="_517" tabindex="-1">

2207Diagnosis of PHACE syndrome requires clinical examination, cardiac evaluation with echocardiogram, ophthalmologic evaluation, and MRI/magnetic resonance angiogram (MRA) of the head and neck. All patients with intermediate-risk and high-risk central nervous system (CNS) findings should be monitored by a neurologist and/or neurosurgeon. Coarctation of the aorta requires immediate cardiology consultation, and a cardiac MRI/MRA may be warranted. A report of two patients with retro-orbital infantile hemangiomas and arteriopathy suggested a possible new presentation of PHACE syndrome.[<a href="#cit/section_4.56">

220856</a>

2209] For patients with proptosis, globe deviation, and strabismus, an MRI/MRA is recommended. Further workup for PHACE syndrome may be needed on the basis of CNS findings. </p>

2210<p id="_2439" tabindex="-1">

2211Short- and long-term issues related to PHACE syndrome include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.62">

221262</a>

2213-<a href="#cit/section_4.64">

221464</a>

2215]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.65">

221665</a>

2217][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2218Level of evidence C1</a>

2219]</p>

2220<div class="pdq-content-list">

2221<ul id="_2440">

2222<li>

2223Headache.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2224<ul id="_2441">

2225<li>

2226May present at an early age.</li>

2227<li>

2228Can be severe.</li>

2229<li>

2230New-onset headaches should be evaluated for vasculopathy and/or cerebral ischemia.</li>

2231<li>

2232Neurology referral is recommended.</li>

2233<li>

2234Vasoconstrictive medications are contraindicated.</li>

2235</ul>

2236</div>

2237</li>

2238<li>

2239Hearing loss and speech-language delays.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2240<ul id="_2442">

2241<li>

2242Speech-language delays may be a consequence of hearing deficits, prolonged hospitalizations, or may occur because of other neurodevelopmental anomalies.</li>

2243<li>

2244Sensorineural hearing loss is the most common type, and it is usually ipsilateral to the infantile hemangioma, which may involve the ipsilateral cranial nerve VIII.</li>

2245<li>

2246Early detection is crucial.</li>

2247<li>

2248All patients with PHACE syndrome should undergo hearing screening as a newborn and at least one follow-up if initial screening is normal.</li>

2249</ul>

2250</div>

2251</li>

2252<li>

2253Dysphagia, feeding disorders, speech disorders, and/or language delay.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2254<ul id="_2443">

2255<li>

2256Increased in patients with posterior fossa malformations, lip/oropharynx or airway hemangiomas, hearing loss, and those with a history of cardiac surgery.</li>

2257<li>

2258Patients should undergo an initial speech language evaluation before age 24 months.</li>

2259<li>

2260Patients with feeding difficulties should be referred for evaluation by a pediatric speech-language pathologist at any age.</li>

2261<li>

2262Dysphagia may be secondary to the disease location (lip, oral cavity, and pharynx) or oral motor coordination.</li>

2263</ul>

2264</div>

2265</li>

2266<li>

2267Endocrine abnormalities.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2268<ul id="_2444">

2269<li>

2270Thyroid dysfunction and hypopituitarism resulting in growth hormone deficiency are the most frequently reported abnormalities.</li>

2271<li>

2272Other manifestations of hypopituitarism, including hypogonadotropic hypogonadism and adrenal insufficiency, have been described.</li>

2273<li>

2274Patients should undergo neonatal screening and repeat studies if symptomatic.</li>

2275<li>

2276Growth hormone deficiency: Most reported cases are associated with hypopituitarism with empty or partially empty sella turcica noted on MRI, but it may also occur without evidence of central nervous system malformations.</li>

2277<li>

2278Neonatal hypoglycemia can be a sign of hypopituitarism and should prompt additional endocrinologic evaluation.</li>

2279<li>

2280Other consequences of pituitary dysfunction include hypogonadotropic hypogonadism, manifesting with delayed pubertal onset and late-onset adrenal insufficiency. These findings emphasize the importance of focused assessment of height, weight, and developmental milestones in the care of children with PHACE.</li>

2281</ul>

2282</div>

2283</li>

2284<li>

2285Dental abnormalities (enamel hypoplasia).<div class="pdq-content-list">

2286<ul id="_2445">

2287<li>

2288A study of 18 children with PHACE or possible PHACE syndrome revealed that 28% of patients had enamel hypoplasia. All of the affected children had intraoral hemangiomas. Five of 11 (45%) patients with intraoral hemangiomas had enamel defects. Children with enamel hypoplasia are at increased risk of developing caries. </li>

2289<li>

2290Patients should be examined for the presence of intraoral hemangiomas. If they are present, patients should be referred to a pediatric dentist by age 1 year for early screening and management.</li>

2291</ul>

2292</div>

2293</li>

2294</ul>

2295</div>

2296</section>

2297<section id="_521">

2298<h5 id="_521_toc">

2299LUMBAR/PELVIS/SACRAL syndrome</h5>

2300<p id="_522" tabindex="-1">

2301 Infantile hemangiomas located over the lumbar or sacral spine may be associated with genitourinary, anorectal anomalies, or neurological issues such as tethered cord.[<a href="#cit/section_4.66">

230266</a>

2303-<a href="#cit/section_4.69">

230469</a>

2305] The following criteria have been used to describe segmental infantile hemangioma syndrome in the lumbar, pelvic, and sacral areas. This syndrome has been described in the literature using several acronyms.</p>

2306<p id="_2456" tabindex="-1">

2307<strong>

2308LUMBAR</strong>

2309</p>

2310<div class="pdq-content-list">

2311<ul id="_523" class="list-dash">

2312<li>

2313<strong>

2314L</strong>

2315ower-body hemangiomas and other cutaneous defects.</li>

2316<li>

2317<strong>

2318U</strong>

2319rogenital anomalies or ulceration.</li>

2320<li>

2321<strong>

2322M</strong>

2323yelopathy.</li>

2324<li>

2325<strong>

2326B</strong>

2327ony deformities.</li>

2328<li>

2329<strong>

2330A</strong>

2331norectal malformations or arterial anomalies.</li>

2332<li>

2333<strong>

2334R</strong>

2335enal anomalies.</li>

2336</ul>

2337</div>

2338<p id="_2457" tabindex="-1">

2339<strong>

2340PELVIS</strong>

2341</p>

2342<div class="pdq-content-list">

2343<ul id="_524" class="list-dash">

2344<li>

2345<strong>

2346P</strong>

2347erineal hemangiomas.</li>

2348<li>

2349<strong>

2350E</strong>

2351xternal genital malformations.</li>

2352<li>

2353<strong>

2354L</strong>

2355ipomyelomeningocele.</li>

2356<li>

2357<strong>

2358V</strong>

2359esicorenal abnormalities.</li>

2360<li>

2361<strong>

2362I</strong>

2363mperforate anus.</li>

2364<li>

2365<strong>

2366S</strong>

2367kin tag.</li>

2368</ul>

2369</div>

2370<p id="_2458" tabindex="-1">

2371<strong>

2372SACRAL</strong>

2373</p>

2374<div class="pdq-content-list">

2375<ul id="_525" class="list-dash">

2376<li>

2377<strong>

2378S</strong>

2379pinal dysraphism.</li>

2380<li>

2381<strong>

2382A</strong>

2383nogenital.</li>

2384<li>

2385<strong>

2386C</strong>

2387utaneous.</li>

2388<li>

2389<strong>

2390R</strong>

2391enal and urologic anomalies <strong>

2392A</strong>

2393ssociated with an angioma of <strong>

2394L</strong>

2395umbosacral localization.</li>

2396</ul>

2397</div>

2398<p id="_526" tabindex="-1">

2399Segmental lesions over the gluteal cleft and lumbar spine need to be evaluated with either ultrasonography or MRI, depending on the age of the patient. In several studies, ultrasonography evaluations have failed to identify some spinal abnormalities that were later found on MRI evaluation.[<a href="#cit/section_4.70">

240070</a>

2401,<a href="#cit/section_4.71">

240271</a>

2403]</p>

2404</section>

2405</section>

2406<section id="_405">

2407<h4 id="_405_toc">

2408Multiple hemangiomas</h4>

2409<p id="_197" tabindex="-1">

2410Infants with more than five hemangiomas need to be evaluated for visceral hemangiomas. The most common site of involvement is the liver, in which multiple or diffuse lesions can be noted.[<a href="#cit/section_4.72">

241172</a>

2412-<a href="#cit/section_4.74">

241374</a>

2414] Often these lesions are asymptomatic, but in a minority of cases, symptoms such as heart failure secondary to large vessel shunts, compartment syndrome, or profound hypothyroidism can occur because of the expression of iodothyronine deiodinase by the hemangioma cells.[<a href="#cit/section_4.75">

241575</a>

2416] Multiple or diffuse liver hemangiomas can occur in the absence of skin lesions.

2417Other rare potential complications of visceral hemangiomas depend on specific organ involvement and are caused by mass effects. These complications include gastrointestinal hemorrhage, obstructive jaundice, and CNS sequelae.

2418For more information, see the <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_134">

2419Benign Vascular Tumors of the Liver</a>

2420 section.</p>

2421</section>

2422<section id="_19">

2423<h4 id="_19_toc">

2424Treatment of infantile hemangioma</h4>

2425<p id="_406" tabindex="-1">

2426The decision to treat patients with hemangiomas is based on several factors, including the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.76">

242776</a>

2428]</p>

2429<div class="pdq-content-list">

2430<ul id="_2459">

2431<li>

2432 Size of the lesions.</li>

2433<li>

2434Type of hemangioma.</li>

2435<li>

2436Location of hemangioma.</li>

2437<li>

2438Presence or risk of complications, including ulceration, possibility of scarring or disfigurement, the age of the patient, and the stage of growth of the hemangioma. </li>

2439</ul>

2440</div>

2441<p id="_2460" tabindex="-1">

2442This decision is individualized among patients, and it is important to carefully consider the risks and benefits of treatment. </p>

2443<p id="_407" tabindex="-1">

2444The American Academy of Pediatrics has published clinical practice guidelines on this topic. An early therapeutic intervention was noted to be critical for complex infantile hemangiomas to prevent medical complications and permanent disfigurement. The timing of interventions was noted to be best in the first 1 to 3 months of age. Photos were used to triage low-risk versus high-risk infantile hemangiomas,[<a href="#cit/section_4.77">

244577</a>

2446] and a scoring system was used for primary care physicians to encourage early referral to hemangioma specialists.[<a href="#cit/section_4.78">

244778</a>

2448] The guidelines indicated that hemangioma specialists are practitioners with expertise in the management and care of hemangiomas who have knowledge of risk stratification and treatment options. These providers consisted of experts in the fields of dermatology, hematology/oncology, pediatrics, plastic surgery, general surgery, otolaryngology, and ophthalmology.[<a href="#cit/section_4.79">

244979</a>

2450]</p>

2451<p id="_20" tabindex="-1">

2452Treatment options for infantile hemangioma include the following:</p>

2453<div class="pdq-content-list">

2454<ol id="_21">

2455<li>

2456<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_128">

2457Propranolol therapy</a>

2458.</li>

2459<li>

2460<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_129">

2461Selective and other beta-blocker therapy</a>

2462.</li>

2463<li>

2464<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_213">

2465Corticosteroid therapy</a>

2466.</li>

2467<li>

2468Laser therapy. <div class="pdq-content-list">

2469<ul id="_2425">

2470<li>

2471Usually reserved for ulcerated infantile hemangiomas and residual lesions, such as telangiectasias after the proliferative period.[<a href="#cit/section_4.80">

247280</a>

2473] Pulsed dye laser therapy helps with pain from ulcerative infantile hemangiomas. The use of pulsed dye laser therapy as an up-front treatment for infantile hemangiomas is controversial.</li>

2474<li>

2475A Russian pilot study employed multiline laser equipment using the Nd:YAP Q-Sw/KTP emitters combined with two wavelengths of 1079/540 nm to treat patients with infantile hemangiomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.81">

247681</a>

2477] Laser treatment was performed on 109 patients with 119 hemangiomas. Evaluation of posttreatment samples revealed restoration of normal color, skin relief, and the absence of scars.</li>

2478<li>

2479A retrospective study in China included 180 patients with superficial hemangiomas who were treated with a 595-nm pulsed dye laser. The study reported that younger children (aged &lt;2 months) received fewer treatments, had shorter courses of disease, and experienced better effects with fewer adverse events when compared with older children.[<a href="#cit/section_4.82">

248082</a>

2481]</li>

2482</ul>

2483</div>

2484</li>

2485<li>

2486Excisional surgery. With the advent of new medical treatments, the use of surgery is reserved for ulcerated lesions, residual lesions, large periocular lesions that interfere with vision, and facial lesions with aesthetic impact that do not respond to medical therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.83">

248783</a>

2488]</li>

2489<li>

2490<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_239">

2491Topical beta-blocker therapy</a>

2492.</li>

2493<li>

2494<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_229">

2495Combined therapy for complicated hemangiomas</a>

2496.</li>

2497</ol>

2498</div>

2499<section id="_128">

2500<h5 id="_128_toc">

2501Propranolol therapy</h5>

2502<p id="_22" tabindex="-1">

2503Propranolol, a nonselective beta-blocker, is first-line therapy for infantile hemangiomas. Early studies suggested that propranolol might act through inducing vasoconstriction and/or by decreasing expression of VEGF and bFGF, leading to apoptosis.[<a href="#cit/section_4.84">

250484</a>

2505,<a href="#cit/section_4.85">

250685</a>

2507] Subsequent studies indicate that the activity of propranolol for infantile hemangiomas is not secondary to beta blockade resulting from action of the S(-) enantiomer of propranolol but rather resulting from the ability of the R(+) enantiomer of propranolol to inhibit SOX18, a transcription factor that acts as a master regulator of vasculogenesis.[<a href="#cit/section_4.86">

250886</a>

2509-<a href="#cit/section_4.88">

251088</a>

2511] The R(+) enantiomer interferes with transcriptional activation by SOX18, disrupts SOX18-chromatin binding dynamics, and inhibits SOX18 dimer formation. These biochemical effects result in inhibition of hemangioma stem cell differentiation into endothelial cells and in inhibition of vasculogenesis.[<a href="#cit/section_4.88">

251288</a>

2513]</p>

2514<p id="_23" tabindex="-1">

2515The use of propranolol was first noted in two infants treated for cardiac issues in Europe. A change in color, softening, and decrease in hemangioma size was noted. Since that time, the results of a randomized controlled trial have been reported.[<a href="#cit/section_4.89">

251689</a>

2517] In 2014, the U.S. Food and Drug Administration (FDA) approved Hemangeol, the pediatric formulation of propranolol hydrochloride, for the treatment of proliferating infantile hemangiomas. Generic propranolol remains in common use.</p>

2518<p id="_27" tabindex="-1">

2519There are many other published reports about the efficacy and safety of propranolol.[<a href="#cit/section_4.90">

252090</a>

2521-<a href="#cit/section_4.94">

252294</a>

2523] Lack of response to treatment is rare. Propranolol therapy is usually used during the proliferative phase but has been effective in patients older than 12 months with infantile hemangiomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.95">

252495</a>

2525]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.96">

252696</a>

2527][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2528Level of evidence C3</a>

2529]</p>

2530<p id="_24" tabindex="-1">

2531Evidence (propranolol therapy):</p>

2532<div class="pdq-content-list">

2533<ol id="_25">

2534<li>

2535In a large industry-sponsored randomized trial, 456 infants aged 5 weeks to 5 months with proliferating infantile hemangiomas of at least 1.5 cm received either a placebo or propranolol (1 mg/kg per day or 3 mg/kg per day) for 3 or 6 months. After interim analysis of the first 188 patients who completed 24 weeks of trial treatment, the regimen of 3 mg/kg per day for 6 months was selected for the final efficacy analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_4.89">

253689</a>

2537][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810031&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810031&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2538Level of evidence B3</a>

2539]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2540<ul id="_26">

2541<li>

2542Of patients who received the selected regimen, 88% showed improvement by week 5, compared with 5% of patients who received the placebo.</li>

2543<li>

2544Adverse events occurred infrequently.</li>

2545</ul>

2546</div>

2547</li>

2548<li>

2549A retrospective study of 635 infants with infantile hemangiomas who were treated with propranolol (2 mg/kg per day) had the following results:[<a href="#cit/section_4.94">

255094</a>

2551][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2552Level of evidence C3</a>

2553] <div class="pdq-content-list">

2554<ul id="_435">

2555<li>

2556The overall response rate was 91%, with most patients demonstrating regression.</li>

2557<li>

2558Two percent of patients had side effects, none of which were severe.</li>

2559</ul>

2560</div>

2561</li>

2562<li>

2563A meta-analysis that evaluated 5,130 patients from 61 studies concluded that propranolol was more effective and safer than were other treatments for infantile hemangioma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.97">

256497</a>

2565]</li>

2566<li>

2567Airway infantile hemangioma lesions are rare. A meta-analysis of 61 patients reported the following results:[<a href="#cit/section_4.98">

256898</a>

2569]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2570<ul id="_436">

2571<li>

2572There was a trend in decreased treatment failure with increased dosing strategies, which is consistent with the use of higher doses of propranolol in these patients (3 mg/kg per day).</li>

2573<li>

2574The analysis also suggested that the concurrent use of steroids and propranolol may have reduced efficacy in patients with segmental airway hemangiomas, but prior treatment with steroids had no deleterious effect.</li>

2575<li>

2576Additional prospective studies are needed to validate these findings.</li>

2577<li>

2578Diffuse (segmental) hemangiomas of the airway are very rare, and their clinical behavior is different from that of isolated airway lesions.</li>

2579</ul>

2580</div>

2581</li>

2582</ol>

2583</div>

2584<p id="_481" tabindex="-1">

2585 Intralesional administration of propranolol has been used for periorbital lesions in a limited capacity and showed no advantages over oral administration.[<a href="#cit/section_4.99">

258699</a>

2587][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810031&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810031&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2588Level of evidence B3</a>

2589]</p>

2590<p id="_408" tabindex="-1">

2591Several expert consensus panel recommendations have been reported, including recommendations from the FDA and the European Medicines Agency after a randomized controlled trial of oral propranolol in infantile hemangioma patients led to FDA approval.[<a href="#cit/section_4.100">

2592100</a>

2593-<a href="#cit/section_4.102">

2594102</a>

2595]</p>

2596<p id="_411" tabindex="-1">

2597Considerations for the use of propranolol include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.100">

2598100</a>

2599,<a href="#cit/section_4.102">

2600102</a>

2601,<a href="#cit/section_4.103">

2602103</a>

2603]</p>

2604<div class="pdq-content-list">

2605<ul id="_29">

2606<li>

2607<strong>

2608Initiation of treatment:</strong>

2609 Guidance from consensus panels suggested that treatment should be undertaken in consultation with a pediatric vascular anomaly specialist with expertise in the diagnosis and treatment of pediatric vascular tumors and in the use of propranolol in children. They suggested that hospitalization for initiation of oral propranolol be considered in the following circumstances:[<a href="#cit/section_4.100">

2610100</a>

2611]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2612<ul id="_30" class="list-dash">

2613<li>

2614Infant aged 4 weeks or younger (corrected for gestational age).</li>

2615<li>

2616Infant of any age with inadequate social support.</li>

2617<li>

2618Infant of any age with comorbid conditions affecting the cardiovascular or respiratory system, including symptomatic airway infantile hemangiomas.</li>

2619<li>

2620Infant of any age with conditions affecting blood glucose maintenance.</li>

2621</ul>

2622</div>

2623<p id="_42" tabindex="-1">

2624The pretreatment evaluation (inpatient or outpatient) includes the following:</p>

2625<div class="pdq-content-list">

2626<ul id="_31" class="list-dash">

2627<li>

2628History, with focus on cardiovascular and respiratory abnormalities (e.g., poor feeding, dyspnea, tachypnea, diaphoresis, wheezing, heart murmur) and family history of heart block or arrhythmia.</li>

2629<li>

2630Physical examination, including cardiac and pulmonary assessment and measurement of heart rate.</li>

2631<li>

2632No need for echocardiogram or electrocardiogram for standard-risk patients. Two studies found no contraindication to beta-blocker therapy in 6.5% to 25% of patients who had electrocardiogram abnormalities.[<a href="#cit/section_4.103">

2633103</a>

2634,<a href="#cit/section_4.104">

2635104</a>

2636] Electrocardiogram should be considered in children with heart rate lower than normal for age and history of arrhythmia or arrhythmia detected during examination.</li>

2637<li>

2638Family history of congenital heart disease or maternal history of connective tissue disease.</li>

2639</ul>

2640</div>

2641</li>

2642<li>

2643<strong>

2644Dosing:</strong>

2645 According to the consensus panels, the dosing used is generally 1 mg/kg per day to 3 mg/kg per day divided into two or three doses. The starting dose varies depending on risk factors and location of initiation. Outpatients and inpatients are initially started at a dose of 0.5 mg/kg per day to 1 mg/kg per day and increased over time.[<a href="#cit/section_4.101">

2646101</a>

2647-<a href="#cit/section_4.103">

2648103</a>

2649] Initially, dosing of three times per day is recommended for infants younger than 5 weeks and for patients with PHACE syndrome.[<a href="#cit/section_4.45">

265045</a>

2651,<a href="#cit/section_4.100">

2652100</a>

2653]</li>

2654<li>

2655<strong>

2656Monitoring:</strong>

2657 Monitoring varies depending on the institution. However, oral propranolol peaks at 1 to 3 hours after administration and most centers measure heart rate and blood pressure 1 and 2 hours after each dose with initiation and then when the dose is increased by at least 0.5 mg/kg per day. Parent and patient education includes when to withhold the medication, signs of hypoglycemia, feeding necessity through the night, and when to call the physician with issues, such as illness, that may interfere with oral intake or lead to dehydration or respiratory problems.<p id="_474" tabindex="-1">

2658A large retrospective multicenter study assessed the safety of outpatient administration of propranolol and evaluated the need for monitoring. In this study, 783 patients with 1,148 office visits were evaluated. No symptomatic bradycardia or hypotension was noted. Blood pressure evaluation was unreliable. The results suggested that outpatient evaluation may not be necessary for standard-risk patients with infantile hemangiomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.105">

2659105</a>

2660]</p>

2661</li>

2662<li>

2663<strong>

2664Contraindications:</strong>

2665 Propranolol treatment is contraindicated in infants and children with the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.100">

2666100</a>

2667-<a href="#cit/section_4.102">

2668102</a>

2669]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2670<ul id="_32" class="list-dash">

2671<li>

2672Sinus bradycardia.</li>

2673<li>

2674Hypotension.</li>

2675<li>

2676Heart block greater than first degree.</li>

2677<li>

2678Heart failure.</li>

2679<li>

2680Asthma.</li>

2681<li>

2682Hypersensitivity.</li>

2683<li>

2684PHACE syndrome. PHACE syndrome with CNS arterial disease and/or coarctation of the aorta may be a relative contraindication. A retrospective multi-institutional study that investigated the safety of propranolol therapy for patients with PHACE syndrome identified 76 infants, including 12 patients who were at high risk of having a stroke.[<a href="#cit/section_4.106">

2685106</a>

2686] The incidence of adverse events in these patients was similar to the incidence in 726 infants who received oral propranolol therapy for hemangioma but did not meet the criteria for PHACE syndrome. A decision to treat should be made in consultation with neurology/neurosurgery and cardiology.

2687</li>

2688</ul>

2689</div>

2690</li>

2691<li>

2692<strong>

2693Adverse effects</strong>

2694 of propranolol include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.107">

2695107</a>

2696]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2697<ul id="_33" class="list-dash">

2698<li>

2699Hypoglycemia.<p id="_2424" tabindex="-1">

2700One study in Japan monitored hypoglycemia in infants with infantile hemangiomas who started treatment with propranolol.[<a href="#cit/section_4.108">

2701108</a>

2702] After treatment with propranolol, the incidences of severe hypoglycemia and hypoglycemic convulsions were approximately 0.54% and 0.35%, respectively. The incidence of hypoglycemic convulsions appeared to be higher in Japan than in Western countries. Severe hypoglycemia was common in infants younger than 1 year when propranolol was used for 6 months or longer. Severe hypoglycemia often developed from 5:00 AM to 9:00 AM, and it was frequently associated with prolonged periods of fasting, poor feeding, or poor physical conditions.</p>

2703</li>

2704<li>

2705Hypotension.</li>

2706<li>

2707Bradycardia.</li>

2708<li>

2709Sleep disturbance.</li>

2710<li>

2711Diarrhea/constipation.</li>

2712<li>

2713Cold extremities.

2714</li>

2715</ul>

2716</div>

2717<p id="_212" tabindex="-1">

2718These complications have been reported in several studies, and severe complications have been rare.[<a href="#cit/section_4.107">

2719107</a>

2720,<a href="#cit/section_4.109">

2721109</a>

2722] The risk of these complications is increased in patients with comorbidities and concomitant diseases, including diarrhea, vomiting, and respiratory infections. The need for close monitoring and possible periods of drug discontinuation should be considered during periods of illness.</p>

2723<p id="_382" tabindex="-1">

2724A retrospective review of 1,260 children with infantile hemangiomas who were treated with propranolol identified 26 patients (2.1%) with side effects that required discontinuation of propranolol.[<a href="#cit/section_4.110">

2725110</a>

2726] Severe sleep disturbance was the most common reason for propranolol cessation, accounting for 65.4% of cases. In total, 23 patients received atenolol and 3 patients received prednisolone as second-line therapy. In the multivariate analysis, only younger age (95% confidence interval [CI], 1.201&#8211;2.793; <em>

2727P</em>

2728&#8201;=&#8201;.009) and lower body weight (95% CI, 1.036&#8211;1.972; <em>

2729P</em>

2730&#8201;=&#8201;.014) were associated with intolerable side effects.</p>

2731</li>

2732<li>

2733<strong>

2734Duration of treatment:</strong>

2735 There are no consensus guidelines for the treatment duration of propranolol. In a prospective, multi-institutional study that assessed efficacy and safety of propranolol in high-risk patients, the administration of propranolol for a minimum of 6 months, up to a maximum of age 12 months, increased treatment success; dosing of propranolol was 3 mg/kg per day. Treatment results were sustained for up to 3 months after discontinuation of therapy. Efficacy and safety of propranolol in this study were similar to those reported in other studies.[<a href="#cit/section_4.111">

2736111</a>

2737]</li>

2738<li>

2739<strong>

2740Rebound growth after propranolol therapy:</strong>

2741 Rebound refers to the growth of infantile hemangiomas after propranolol cessation. A multi-institutional, retrospective review of 997 patients with infantile hemangiomas found a rebound rate of 25.3% in 912 patients with adequate data. On univariate analysis, the factors associated with rebound included discontinuation of treatment before age 9 months, female sex, location on the head or neck, segmental pattern, and deep or mixed skin involvement. On multivariate analysis, only deep infantile hemangiomas and female sex were significantly related.[<a href="#cit/section_4.112">

2742112</a>

2743] A single-center retrospective review examined 198 patients with infantile hemangioma who underwent oral propranolol therapy. The study reported 35 patients (18%) with rebound growth 1 to 3 months after discontinuation of propranolol treatment. Of the 35 patients, 23 were re-treated with propranolol for up to 3 months. All patients had good responses.[<a href="#cit/section_4.113">

2744113</a>

2745][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2746Level of evidence C3</a>

2747]</li>

2748<li>

2749<strong>

2750Late growth of infantile hemangiomas:</strong>

2751 Hemangioma growth can occur in patients older than 3 years, and growth as late as age 8.5 years has been reported. Associated risk factors include segmental morphology, large hemangiomas, PHACE syndrome, and deep cutaneous and subcutaneous lesions in the head and neck.[<a href="#cit/section_4.114">

2752114</a>
