2753,<a href="#cit/section_4.115">

2754115</a>

2755]</li>

2756</ul>

2757</div>

2758</section>

2759<section id="_129">

2760<h5 id="_129_toc">

2761Selective and other beta-blocker therapy</h5>

2762<p id="_130" tabindex="-1">

2763Because of the nonselective and lipophilic nature of propranolol and its ability to cross the blood-brain barrier, other beta-blockers are being used for the treatment of infantile hemangiomas.</p>

2764<p id="_437" tabindex="-1">

2765Evidence (beta-blocker therapy):</p>

2766<div class="pdq-content-list">

2767<ol id="_438">

2768<li>

2769 In two small comparison studies, there was no difference in efficacy between propranolol and atenolol.[<a href="#cit/section_4.116">

2770116</a>

2771,<a href="#cit/section_4.117">

2772117</a>

2773]</li>

2774<li>

2775In support of a previous retrospective study, a prospective double-blind study compared nadolol with propranolol in 71 infants (aged 1&#8211;6 months).[<a href="#cit/section_4.118">

2776118</a>

2777][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810021&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810021&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2778Level of evidence A3</a>

2779]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2780<ul id="_2416">

2781<li>

2782The study demonstrated noninferiority with respect to efficacy and treatment.</li>

2783</ul>

2784</div>

2785</li>

2786<li>

2787 A prospective study of 76 infants treated with atenolol noted efficacy and safety similar to propranolol.[<a href="#cit/section_4.119">

2788119</a>

2789][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2790Level of evidence C3</a>

2791]</li>

2792</ol>

2793</div>

2794<p id="_2452" tabindex="-1">

2795In one published report, nadolol was associated with the death of an infant (aged 17 weeks) after 10 days of no stool output.[<a href="#cit/section_4.120">

2796120</a>

2797] There is limited information about the pharmacokinetics and safety of nadolol in infants. The drug has a narrow therapeutic index, and it is excreted and remains unchanged in the feces. If an infant is given nadolol, it is critical to monitor for regular stool output.</p>

2798<p id="_439" tabindex="-1">

2799Additional studies are needed to assess differences between the toxicities of these agents and the toxicities of propranolol. </p>

2800<p id="_412" tabindex="-1">

2801 There is some suggestion that the more selective beta-blockers have fewer side effects.[<a href="#cit/section_4.121">

2802121</a>

2803] A study has suggested that the R(+) enantiomer of propranolol, carried over in drug synthesis rather than the anti&#8211;beta-adrenergic L(-) enantiomer (commercially available drug is a racemic mixture), may carry the therapeutic anti-infantile hemangioma effect.[<a href="#cit/section_4.86">

280486</a>

2805,<a href="#cit/section_4.87">

280687</a>

2807]</p>

2808</section>

2809<section id="_213">

2810<h5 id="_213_toc">

2811Corticosteroid therapy</h5>

2812<p id="_214" tabindex="-1">

2813Before propranolol, corticosteroids were the first line of treatment for infantile hemangiomas. They were first used in the late 1950s but were never approved by the U.S. FDA for this indication. Corticosteroid therapy has become less popular because of the acute and long-term side effects of steroids (gastrointestinal irritability, immunosuppression, adrenocortical suppression, cushingoid features, and growth failure). </p>

2814<p id="_215" tabindex="-1">

2815Corticosteroids (prednisone or methylprednisolone) are used at times when there is a contraindication to beta-blocker therapy or as initial treatment while a patient is started on beta-blocker therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.122">

2816122</a>

2817]</p>

2818</section>

2819<section id="_239">

2820<h5 id="_239_toc">

2821Topical beta-blocker therapy</h5>

2822<p id="_257" tabindex="-1">

2823Topical beta-blockers are used mainly for the treatment of small, localized, superficial hemangiomas as an alternative to observation. They have also been used in combination with systemic therapy in complicated hemangiomas or to prevent rebound in hemangiomas being tapered off of systemic treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_4.123">

2824123</a>

2825-<a href="#cit/section_4.125">

2826125</a>

2827] The same precautions (assessment of comorbidities and family history), as noted previously for propranolol, should be followed for topical beta-blockers. Systemic absorption (plasma and urine) of timolol is variable and prescreening for normal cardiac, pulmonary, and endocrine issues are essential. Recent medical histories and physical examinations are also important. Cautious administration is necessary for ulcerated and deep hemangiomas because higher plasma concentrations of timolol can be seen.[<a href="#cit/section_4.126">

2828126</a>

2829,<a href="#cit/section_4.127">

2830127</a>

2831]</p>

2832<p id="_305" tabindex="-1">

2833The topical timolol that is used is the ophthalmic gel-forming solution 0.5%. One drop is applied to the hemangioma two times per day until stable response is achieved.</p>

2834<p id="_240" tabindex="-1">

2835This treatment has limited side effects, but infants with a postmenstrual age of younger than 44 weeks and weight at treatment initiation of less than 2,500 grams may be at risk of adverse events, including bradycardia, hypotension, apnea, and hypothermia.[<a href="#cit/section_4.127">

2836127</a>

2837,<a href="#cit/section_4.128">

2838128</a>

2839] Close monitoring of temperature, blood pressure, and heart rate in premature and low birth weight infants with infantile hemangiomas at initiation of and during therapy with topical timolol is necessary.</p>

2840<p id="_241" tabindex="-1">

2841Evidence (topical timolol therapy):</p>

2842<div class="pdq-content-list">

2843<ol id="_242">

2844<li>

2845In a multicenter, retrospective, cohort study, 731 children with predominantly superficial hemangiomas were treated with topical timolol 0.5% twice daily.[<a href="#cit/section_4.125">

2846125</a>

2847]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2848<ul id="_496">

2849<li>

2850 Ninety-two percent of patients showed significant improvement in hemangioma color. </li>

2851<li>

2852Seventy-seven percent of patients showed improvement in hemangioma size, extent, and volume.</li>

2853<li>

2854Topical timolol is generally well tolerated; however, data on its safety are limited.</li>

2855</ul>

2856</div>

2857</li>

2858<li>

2859A Spanish consortium performed a prospective randomized trial to evaluate the efficacy and safety of topical timolol for the treatment of infantile hemangioma in the early proliferative stage.[<a href="#cit/section_4.129">

2860129</a>

2861] This multicenter, randomized, double-blind, placebo-controlled, phase IIa pilot clinical trial included patients aged 10 to 60 days with focal or segmental hemangiomas (superficial, deep, mixed, or minimal/arrested growth). Patients were randomly assigned to treatment with either topical timolol maleate solution, 0.5%, or placebo, twice daily for 24 weeks. <div class="pdq-content-list">

2862<ul id="_2394">

2863<li>

2864At 24 weeks, there were no significant differences between the timolol treatment and the placebo for complete or nearly complete infantile hemangioma resolution (42% for timolol [n = 11] vs. 36% for placebo [n = 11]; <em>

2865P</em>

2866 = .37).</li>

2867</ul>

2868</div>

2869</li>

2870</ol>

2871</div>

2872</section>

2873<section id="_229">

2874<h5 id="_229_toc">

2875Combined therapy for complicated hemangiomas</h5>

2876<p id="_258" tabindex="-1">

2877Combined therapy is considered either at initiation of treatment in complicated lesions in which there is functional impairment or organ compromise or used at the end of systemic therapy to prevent hemangioma rebound. Further investigation of efficacy and safety is needed for these regimens.</p>

2878<p id="_259" tabindex="-1">

2879Evidence (combined therapy for complicated hemangiomas):</p>

2880<div class="pdq-content-list">

2881<ol id="_260">

2882<li>

2883A prospective randomized study that compared propranolol and 2 weeks of steroid therapy with propranolol alone revealed the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.130">

2884130</a>

2885]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2886<ul id="_494">

2887<li>

2888Decreased sizes of hemangiomas at 2, 4, and 8 weeks in the combined-therapy group but no statistical difference in the sizes at 6 months.</li>

2889</ul>

2890</div>

2891</li>

2892<li>

2893 A prospective randomized study that compared timolol and propranolol with propranolol alone reported the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.131">

2894131</a>

2895]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2896<ul id="_495">

2897<li>

2898Decreased color of the infantile hemangiomas in the timolol group but no difference in overall sizes of the infantile hemangiomas between the two treatment groups.</li>

2899<li>

2900Other studies have supported this combination.[<a href="#cit/section_4.132">

2901132</a>

2902,<a href="#cit/section_4.133">

2903133</a>

2904][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2905Level of evidence C3</a>

2906]</li>

2907</ul>

2908</div>

2909</li>

2910</ol>

2911</div>

2912</section>

2913</section>

2914<section id="_261">

2915<h4 id="_261_toc">

2916Treatment options under clinical evaluation for infantile hemangiomas</h4>

2917<p id="_262" tabindex="-1">

2918Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

2919NCI website</a>

2920. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

2921ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

2922.</p>

2923<p id="_432" tabindex="-1">

2924In response to the COVID-19 pandemic, the Hemangioma Investigator Group is studying the administration of propranolol for low-risk and standard-risk patients through virtual visits.[<a href="#cit/section_4.134">

2925134</a>

2926]</p>

2927<section id="_TrialSearch_261_sid_9">

2928<h5 id="_TrialSearch_261_sid_9_toc">

2929Current Clinical Trials</h5>

2930<p id="_TrialSearch_261_22" tabindex="-1">

2931Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

2932advanced clinical trial search</a>

2933 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

2934General information</a>

2935 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

2936</section>

2937</section>

2938</section>

2939<section id="_131">

2940<h3 id="_131_toc">

2941Congenital Hemangiomas</h3>

2942<section id="_458">

2943<h4 id="_458_toc">

2944Clinical features and diagnostic evaluation</h4>

2945<p id="_297" tabindex="-1">

2946Congenital hemangiomas can be difficult to diagnose, especially for clinicians who are unfamiliar with these lesions. Diagnostic criteria include a purpuric lesion fully formed at birth, frequently with a halo around the lesion, with high flow noted on ultrasound imaging. Essential to the diagnosis is serial observation for decrease or, at least stability, in size over time. These lesions do not enlarge unless there is hemorrhage into the tumor.</p>

2947<p id="_160" tabindex="-1">

2948Congenital hemangiomas are divided into the following three forms:</p>

2949<div class="pdq-content-list">

2950<ul id="_133">

2951<li>

2952<strong>

2953Rapidly involuting congenital hemangiomas (RICH).</strong>

2954 These lesions are large high-flow lesions that are completely formed at birth but rapidly involute by age 12 to 15 months. They can ulcerate and bleed and can cause transient heart failure and mild coagulopathy. After involution, usually some residual changes in the skin are present (see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_167">

2955Figure 6</a>

2956).[<a href="#cit/section_4.135">

2957135</a>

2958-<a href="#cit/section_4.138">

2959138</a>

2960]<p id="_440" tabindex="-1">

2961 In a retrospective case series of congenital hemangiomas, several high-risk ultrasound findings were noted for RICH. Venous lakes were associated with cardiac failure, and an increased risk of bleeding was noted with venous lakes and venous ectasia. Infants with RICH should be evaluated with ultrasonography and monitored closely if these high-risk features are noted.[<a href="#cit/section_4.139">

2962139</a>

2963]</p>

2964<p id="_211" tabindex="-1">

2965<figure id="figure_167" class="image-center">

2966<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/779670.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

2967Enlarge</a>

2968<img id="_167" alt="Photographs showing a cutaneous congenital hemangioma on the inner right thigh at birth (left panel), 1 month (middle panel), and 1 year (right panel)." title="Photographs showing a cutaneous congenital hemangioma on the inner right thigh at birth (left panel), 1 month (middle panel), and 1 year (right panel)." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/779670-750.jpg">

2969<figcaption class="caption-container">

2970Figure 6. Typical appearance of a cutaneous congenital hemangioma at birth. Note the pedunculated mass. This RICH lesion involuted over time but some residual skin changes remained. Credit: Denise Adams, M.D.</figcaption>

2971</figure>

2972</p>

2973</li>

2974<li>

2975<strong>

2976Partial involuting congenital hemangiomas (PICH).</strong>

2977 These lesions are completely formed at birth and involute only partially.[<a href="#cit/section_4.140">

2978140</a>

2979]</li>

2980<li>

2981<strong>

2982Non-involuting congenital hemangiomas (NICH).</strong>

2983 These lesions are formed at birth and never involute. Depending on the location of the lesions and whether they cause functional impairment, the lesions may need to be removed surgically.[<a href="#cit/section_4.141">

2984141</a>

2985,<a href="#cit/section_4.142">

2986142</a>

2987]</li>

2988</ul>

2989</div>

2990</section>

2991<section id="_457">

2992<h4 id="_457_toc">

2993Histopathology and molecular features</h4>

2994<p id="_132" tabindex="-1">

2995Congenital hemangiomas are benign vascular tumors that proliferate <em>

2996in utero</em>

2997. Development of these lesions is complete at birth. Histologically, these lesions are GLUT1 negative, unlike infantile hemangiomas. They are usually cutaneous, but can be found in the viscera. Complications include hemorrhage, transient heart failure, and transient coagulopathy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.143">

2998143</a>

2999]</p>

3000<p id="_264" tabindex="-1">

3001Somatic activating mutations of <em class="gene-name">

3002GNAQ</em>

3003 and <em class="gene-name">

3004GNA11</em>

3005 have been found to be associated with congenital hemangiomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.144">

3006144</a>

3007] Additional research is necessary to assess the significance of these findings, as this may aid in diagnosis and pathophysiology.</p>

3008</section>

3009</section>

3010<section id="_134">

3011<h3 id="_134_toc">

3012Benign Vascular Tumors of the Liver</h3>

3013<p id="_135" tabindex="-1">

3014In the literature, vascular liver tumors are usually classified as liver hemangioendotheliomas, a broad classification no longer in use. </p>

3015<p id="_482" tabindex="-1">

3016On MRI, vascular liver tumors are hyperintense on T2 imaging and hypointense on T1 imaging, with postcontrast imaging demonstrating early peripheral enhancement with eventual diffuse enhancement.[<a href="#cit/section_4.73">

301773</a>

3018] In practice, these tumors are classified according to their clinical characteristics and radiological assessment.</p>

3019<p id="_136" tabindex="-1">

3020Lesions are usually divided into the following three categories:[<a href="#cit/section_4.73">

302173</a>

3022]</p>

3023<div class="pdq-content-list">

3024<ul id="_137">

3025<li>

3026<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_161">

3027Focal vascular lesions (congenital hemangiomas)</a>

3028.</li>

3029<li>

3030<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_266">

3031Multiple liver lesions (infantile hemangiomas)</a>

3032.</li>

3033<li>

3034<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_298">

3035Diffuse liver lesions (infantile hemangiomas)</a>

3036.</li>

3037</ul>

3038</div>

3039<section id="_161">

3040<h4 id="_161_toc">

3041Focal vascular lesions (congenital hemangiomas)</h4>

3042<p id="_139" tabindex="-1">

3043Focal lesions of the liver are usually congenital hemangiomas (RICH or NICH) (see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_169">

3044Figure 7</a>

3045). RICH can present with symptoms of heart failure and mild to moderate coagulopathy but are typically detected by antenatal ultrasonography or as an asymptomatic mass in the newborn period.</p>

3046<p id="_295" tabindex="-1">

3047Treatment options for focal vascular lesions of the liver include the following:</p>

3048<div class="pdq-content-list">

3049<ol id="_296">

3050<li>

3051Supportive management. Most lesions are asymptomatic and can be monitored through involution using ultrasonography.</li>

3052<li>

3053Embolization. This procedure is considered for severe symptomatic shunting that is unresponsive to treatment for congestive heart failure. These procedures need to be performed by interventional radiologists with expertise in vascular anomalies.[<a href="#cit/section_4.145">

3054145</a>

3055]</li>

3056<li>

3057Surgery. Patients with massive focal symptomatic hepatic congenital hemangioma unresponsive to supportive management or radiological intervention may be surgical candidates for resection. This is a rare circumstance and needs to be evaluated by an interdisciplinary vascular anomaly team. Indication for surgical removal includes rupture, bleeding, and nonresolving coagulopathy. Two patients were reported to require surgical resection after the development of clinically significant ascites as their RICH involuted.[<a href="#cit/section_4.146">

3058146</a>

3059,<a href="#cit/section_4.147">

3060147</a>

3061]</li>

3062</ol>

3063</div>

3064<p id="_265" tabindex="-1">

3065No medication has proven to be an effective treatment for these lesions, and infants need to be supported during this initial period until involution begins.[<a href="#cit/section_4.73">

306673</a>

3067] These lesions may be diagnosed prenatally. In rare situations, maternal treatment with medications such as steroids appeared to be effective but, more likely, natural involution may have been responsible.[<a href="#cit/section_4.148">

3068148</a>

3069]</p>

3070<p id="_168" tabindex="-1">

3071<figure id="figure_169" class="image-center">

3072<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/779672.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

3073Enlarge</a>

3074<img id="_169" alt="MRI image of a single liver lesion (intrahepatic congenital hemangioma)." title="MRI image of a single liver lesion (intrahepatic congenital hemangioma)." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/779672-750.jpg">

3075<figcaption class="caption-container">

3076Figure 7. Single liver lesion (intrahepatic congenital hemangioma). MRI image of a congenital hemangioma. Note the central enhancement, which is typical for an intrahepatic congenital hemangioma. Credit: Denise Adams, M.D.</figcaption>

3077</figure>

3078</p>

3079</section>

3080<section id="_266">

3081<h4 id="_266_toc">

3082Multiple liver lesions (infantile hemangiomas)</h4>

3083<p id="_140" tabindex="-1">

3084Multifocal hepatic lesions are infantile hemangiomas. Multifocal lesions may not need to be treated if the patient is asymptomatic, and they typically follow the same proliferative and involution course as cutaneous hemangiomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.73">

308573</a>

3086] These lesions are monitored closely and if there is growth, propranolol therapy should be considered. If propranolol is needed, doses of up to 2 mg/kg per day are effective.</p>

3087</section>

3088<section id="_298">

3089<h4 id="_298_toc">

3090Diffuse liver lesions (infantile hemangiomas)</h4>

3091<p id="_159" tabindex="-1">

3092Diffuse liver lesions are very serious (see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_171">

3093Figure 8</a>

3094). Complications include hypothyroidism caused by the expression of iodothyronine deiodinase, high-output or congestive heart failure, and abdominal compartment syndrome.[<a href="#cit/section_4.72">

309572</a>

3096,<a href="#cit/section_4.73">

309773</a>

3098,<a href="#cit/section_4.149">

3099149</a>

3100,<a href="#cit/section_4.150">

3101150</a>

3102]</p>

3103<p id="_170" tabindex="-1">

3104<figure id="figure_171" class="image-center">

3105<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/779669.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

3106Enlarge</a>

3107<img id="_171" alt="CT image of diffuse liver lesions." title="CT image of diffuse liver lesions." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/779669-750.jpg">

3108<figcaption class="caption-container">

3109Figure 8. Diffuse liver lesions with classical imaging on CT. Note the peripheral enhancement in early contrast phase. Credit: Denise Adams, M.D.</figcaption>

3110</figure>

3111</p>

3112<p id="_162" tabindex="-1">

3113Treatment options for diffuse liver lesions may include the following:</p>

3114<div class="pdq-content-list">

3115<ol id="_144">

3116<li>

3117<strong>

3118Propranolol:</strong>

3119 Beta-blockers are the most common treatment for diffuse and some multifocal infantile hemangiomas of the liver. Treatment doses of 2 to 3 mg/kg per day are indicated.[<a href="#cit/section_4.89">

312089</a>

3121]</li>

3122<li>

3123<strong>

3124Thyroid hormone replacement:</strong>

3125 Thyroid hormone replacement therapy must be aggressive if hypothyroidism is diagnosed. Treatment with higher doses of hormones may be needed because the deficiency is caused by the aggressive consumption of the hormone by the tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_4.75">

312675</a>

3127]</li>

3128<li>

3129<strong>

3130Chemotherapy:</strong>

3131 Steroids, cyclophosphamide, and vincristine have been used to treat diffuse liver infantile hemangioma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.73">

313273</a>

3133,<a href="#cit/section_4.151">

3134151</a>

3135,<a href="#cit/section_4.152">

3136152</a>

3137]</li>

3138<li>

3139<strong>

3140Transplant:</strong>

3141 If a patient does not respond to medical management, a transplant may be indicated.[<a href="#cit/section_4.153">

3142153</a>

3143] Transplant is considered only for patients with severe diffuse lesions who have multisystem organ failure and there is insufficient time for effective pharmacologic therapy.</li>

3144</ol>

3145</div>

3146<p id="_145" tabindex="-1">

3147There have been isolated reports of malignancy in patients with diffuse hepatic infantile hemangiomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.154">

3148154</a>

3149] It is not clear that all cases were caused by the transformation of a benign lesion to a malignant phenotype. However, if the lesion does not respond to standard therapy, biopsy should be considered. Further evaluation and consensus is needed to assess whether these patients need to be monitored over a longer period of time with liver ultrasonography. For more information, see the <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_192">

3150Angiosarcoma</a>

3151 section.</p>

3152<p id="_267" tabindex="-1">

3153The differential diagnosis of vascular liver lesions always includes malignant liver tumors; thus, alpha-fetoprotein (AFP) should be included in the initial lab work. AFP is very high in all newborns but will rapidly fall to normal levels in several months. AFP levels should rapidly diminish, but failure to do so or a rising trend of AFP should elicit concern for hepatoblastoma. There are no prospective studies investigating AFP elevation in patients with hemangiomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.155">

3154155</a>

3155,<a href="#cit/section_4.156">

3156156</a>

3157] Some hypervascular hepatoblastomas in neonates with congestive heart failure have been mistaken for infantile hemangiomas. Other tumors in the differential diagnosis include angiosarcomas, metastatic neuroblastomas, and mesenchymal hamartomas. If there is any question about the diagnosis, a biopsy is recommended, although bleeding is a risk of the procedure.[<a href="#cit/section_4.157">

3158157</a>

3159]</p>

3160</section>

3161</section>

3162<section id="_43">

3163<h3 id="_43_toc">

3164Spindle Cell Hemangioma</h3>

3165<section id="_44">

3166<h4 id="_44_toc">

3167Clinical presentation, molecular features, and histopathology</h4>

3168<p id="_45" tabindex="-1">

3169Spindle cell hemangiomas, initially called spindle cell hemangioendotheliomas, often occur as superficial (skin and subcutis), painful lesions involving distal extremities in children and adults.[<a href="#cit/section_4.158">

3170158</a>

3171,<a href="#cit/section_4.159">

3172159</a>

3173] The tumors appear as red-brown or bluish lesions that can begin as a single nodule and develop into multifocal painful lesions over years. The hemangiomas are well circumscribed, occasionally contain phleboliths, and consist of cavernous blood spaces alternating with areas of nodular spindle cell proliferation. A significant percentage of spindle cell hemangiomas are completely intravascular. The vein containing the tumor is abnormal, as are blood vessels apart from the tumor mass.[<a href="#cit/section_4.160">

3174160</a>

3175,<a href="#cit/section_4.161">

3176161</a>

3177]</p>

3178<p id="_441" tabindex="-1">

3179Spindle cell hemangiomas can be seen in patients with Maffucci syndrome (cutaneous spindle cell hemangiomas occurring with cartilaginous tumors, enchondromas) and Klippel-Tr&#233;naunay syndrome (capillary/lymphatic/venous malformations), generalized lymphatic anomalies, lymphedema, and organized thrombus.[<a href="#cit/section_4.160">

3180160</a>

3181,<a href="#cit/section_4.161">

3182161</a>

3183] In Maffucci syndrome, spindle cell hemangiomas are associated with <em class="gene-name">

3184IDH1</em>

3185 or <em class="gene-name">

3186IDH2</em>

3187 mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_4.162">

3188162</a>

3189]</p>

3190</section>

3191<section id="_46">

3192<h4 id="_46_toc">

3193Treatment of spindle cell hemangioma</h4>

3194<p id="_47" tabindex="-1">

3195There is no standard treatment for spindle cell hemangioma because it has not been studied in clinical trials. Surgical removal is usually curative, although there is a risk of recurrence.[<a href="#cit/section_4.160">

3196160</a>

3197,<a href="#cit/section_4.161">

3198161</a>

3199]</p>

3200</section>

3201</section>

3202<section id="_48">

3203<h3 id="_48_toc">

3204Epithelioid Hemangioma</h3>

3205<section id="_49">

3206<h4 id="_49_toc">

3207Clinical presentation and histopathology</h4>

3208<p id="_50" tabindex="-1">

3209Epithelioid hemangiomas (EH) are benign lesions that usually occur in the skin and subcutis but can occur in other areas such as the bone, with focal and multifocal lesions.[<a href="#cit/section_4.160">

3210160</a>

3211,<a href="#cit/section_4.163">

3212163</a>

3213] Epithelioid hemangiomas may be a reactive process, as they can be associated with local trauma and can develop in pregnancy. Patients usually present with local swelling and pain at the involved site. In the bone, they present as well-defined lytic lesions that involve the metaphysis and diaphysis of long bones.[<a href="#cit/section_4.160">

3214160</a>

3215,<a href="#cit/section_4.164">

3216164</a>

3217] They can have a mixed lytic and sclerotic pattern of bone destruction. </p>

3218<p id="_51" tabindex="-1">

3219On pathological evaluation, epithelioid hemangiomas have small caliber capillaries with eosinophilic, vacuolated cytoplasm and large oval, grooved, and lobulated nuclei. The endothelial cells are plump and are mature, well-formed vessels surrounded by multiple epithelioid endothelial cells within abundant cytoplasm. They lack cellular atypia and mitotic activity.[<a href="#cit/section_4.160">

3220160</a>

3221,<a href="#cit/section_4.163">

3222163</a>

3223-<a href="#cit/section_4.165">

3224165</a>

3225]</p>

3226<p id="_442" tabindex="-1">

3227In a study of 58 cases of epithelioid hemangiomas, 29% were found to have <em class="gene-name">

3228FOS</em>

3229 gene rearrangements. <em class="gene-name">

3230FOS</em>

3231 gene rearrangements were noted more often in cellular epithelioid hemangiomas and intraosseous lesions compared with lesions in the skin, soft tissue, and head and neck. This genetic abnormality can be helpful in distinguishing epithelioid hemangiomas from other malignant epithelioid vascular tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_4.165">

3232165</a>

3233]</p>

3234<p id="_358" tabindex="-1">

3235A single-institution report reviewed 11 patients with epithelioid hemangiomas (median age, 14.4 years) who were diagnosed between 1999 and 2017. Lesions occurred in the lower extremities (five patients), skull (three patients), pelvis (two patients), and spine (one patient). Five patients had multifocal disease. Patients presented with localized pain and neurological symptoms, including cranial nerve injury. No significant cytological atypia was noted, and the endothelial cells were positive for CD31 and ERG, and negative for cytokeratin and CAMPTA1. Median follow-up was 1.5 years. Various modalities of treatments were used, including surgery, endovascular embolization, cryoablation, and medical management. One patient received sirolimus, and another patient received interferon; the lesions of both patients shrank within the first year of follow-up. The youngest patient, aged 2.5 years, had multifocal skull lesions that partially regressed 1 year later without treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_4.166">

3236166</a>

3237]</p>

3238</section>

3239<section id="_52">

3240<h4 id="_52_toc">

3241Treatment of epithelioid hemangioma</h4>

3242<p id="_53" tabindex="-1">

3243There is no standard treatment for epithelioid hemangioma because it has not been studied in clinical trials. Treatment usually consists of curettage, sclerotherapy, or resection. In rare cases, radiation therapy may be used.[<a href="#cit/section_4.160">

3244160</a>

3245,<a href="#cit/section_4.164">

3246164</a>

3247]</p>

3248</section>

3249</section>

3250<section id="_54">

3251<h3 id="_54_toc">

3252Pyogenic Granuloma (Lobular Capillary Hemangioma)</h3>

3253<section id="_55">

3254<h4 id="_55_toc">

3255Clinical presentation, histopathology, and molecular features</h4>

3256<p id="_56" tabindex="-1">

3257Pyogenic granulomas (PG), known as lobular capillary hemangiomas, are benign reactive lesions. Pyogenic granulomas can present at any age&#8212;including at birth (congenitally), during the neonatal period, during infancy, or during pregnancy&#8212;although they are most common in older children and young adults. These lesions can arise spontaneously, in sites of trauma, or within capillary and arteriovenous malformations. Pyogenic granulomas have also been associated with medications including oral contraceptives and retinoids. </p>

3258<p id="_443" tabindex="-1">

3259Pyogenic granulomas occur as solitary growths, but multiple (grouped) or rarely disseminated lesions have been described.[<a href="#cit/section_4.167">

3260167</a>

3261] These lesions appear as small or large, smooth or lobulated vascular nodules that can grow rapidly, sometimes over weeks to months and have a tendency to bleed profusely. These lesions are usually cutaneous, but deep-seated/subcutaneous pyogenic granulomas have been reported and mimic other vascular lesions.[<a href="#cit/section_4.168">

3262168</a>

3263] Histologically, these lesions are composed of capillaries and venules with plump endothelial cells separated into lobules by fibromyxoid stroma. Some untreated lesions eventually atrophy, become fibromatous, and slowly regress. A retrospective review of a series of eight children with disseminated congenital or neonatal pyogenic granulomas reported the occurrence of hemorrhagic central nervous system lesions in seven patients, five of whom developed neurological sequelae. Four of the eight patients had transient coagulopathy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.169">

3264169</a>

3265][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3266Level of evidence C2</a>

3267]</p>

3268<p id="_428" tabindex="-1">

3269The pathogenesis of pyogenic granulomas associated with capillary malformations and those that are sporadic are unknown. A study investigated ten patients with pyogenic granulomas arising from a capillary malformation and found eight with <em class="gene-name">

3270BRAF</em>

3271 c.1799T&gt;A mutations, one with an <em class="gene-name">

3272NRAS</em>

3273 c.182A&gt;G mutation, and one with a <em class="gene-name">

3274GNAQ</em>

3275 c.548G&gt;A mutation. This <em class="gene-name">

3276GNAQ</em>

3277 mutation was also found in the underlying capillary malformation. In 25 patients with pyogenic granulomas and no capillary malformation, 3 patients had <em class="gene-name">

3278BRAF</em>

3279 c.1799T&gt;A mutations and 1 patient had a <em class="gene-name">

3280KRAS</em>

3281 c.37G&gt;C mutation. These genetic findings will help with future treatment modalities for this benign vascular tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_4.170">

3282170</a>

3283]</p>

3284</section>

3285<section id="_58">

3286<h4 id="_58_toc">

3287Treatment of pyogenic granuloma</h4>

3288<p id="_59" tabindex="-1">

3289Full-thickness excision is the treatment with the lowest recurrence rate (around 3%),[<a href="#cit/section_4.171">

3290171</a>

3291] but curettage, laser photocoagulation, or cryotherapy can also be used.[<a href="#cit/section_4.172">

3292172</a>

3293] Topical timolol and propranolol have also been used.</p>

3294<p id="_444" tabindex="-1">

3295Evidence (topical beta-blockers):</p>

3296<div class="pdq-content-list">

3297<ol id="_445">

3298<li>

3299In a single-arm series of patients with acquired ocular pyogenic granulomas, a small number of pediatric patients were treated for 21 days to 6 weeks with twice-daily topical timolol, 0.5%.[<a href="#cit/section_4.173">

3300173</a>

3301,<a href="#cit/section_4.174">

3302174</a>

3303][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3304Level of evidence C3</a>

3305]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3306<ul id="_528">

3307<li>

3308Complete or near-complete responses without subsequent recurrence or progression were noted in 75% to 100% of the patients (all ages).</li>

3309</ul>

3310</div>

3311</li>

3312<li>

3313A study of 22 patients with cutaneous pyogenic granulomas who were treated with topical 1% propranolol ointment with occlusion had the following results:[<a href="#cit/section_4.175">

3314175</a>

3315]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3316<ul id="_447">

3317<li>

3318Fifty-nine percent of patients achieved complete responses (mean, 66 days), 18% of patients had stable disease, and 22% of patients did not respond to the treatment.</li>

3319<li>

3320 In this study, only skin toxicity was assessed. </li>

3321<li>

3322The authors did not comment on the penetrance of the propranolol formulation or include a safety evaluation of the side effects such as hypoglycemia and the effects on heart rate or blood pressure.</li>

3323</ul>

3324</div>

3325</li>

3326</ol>

3327</div>

3328</section>

3329</section>

3330<section id="_60">

3331<h3 id="_60_toc">

3332Angiofibroma</h3>

3333<section id="_61">

3334<h4 id="_61_toc">

3335Clinical presentation</h4>

3336<p id="_62" tabindex="-1">

3337Angiofibromas are rare, benign neoplasms in the pediatric population. Typically, they are cutaneous lesions associated with tuberous sclerosis, appearing as red papules on the face.</p>

3338</section>

3339<section id="_63">

3340<h4 id="_63_toc">

3341Treatment of angiofibroma</h4>

3342<p id="_64" tabindex="-1">

3343Excision of the tumor, laser treatments, and topical treatments, such as sirolimus, have been used.[<a href="#cit/section_4.176">

3344176</a>

3345-<a href="#cit/section_4.178">

3346178</a>

3347]</p>

3348<p id="_448" tabindex="-1">

3349Evidence (topical sirolimus):</p>

3350<div class="pdq-content-list">

3351<ol id="_449">

3352<li>

3353 A prospective, randomized, placebo-controlled trial of nine cancer centers that included 62 patients who received sirolimus gel demonstrated the following results:[<a href="#cit/section_4.179">

3354179</a>

3355] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3356<ul id="_450">

3357<li>

3358Sixty percent of patients who received sirolimus showed significant improvement in the size and color of the lesion, which was assessed at week 12.</li>

3359</ul>

3360</div>

3361</li>

3362<li>

3363In another prospective, multicenter, randomized, double-blind, vehicle-controlled study that included six monthly clinic visits, 179 patients with tuberous sclerosis complex&#8211;related facial angiofibromas were treated with topical rapamycin (sirolimus) 0.3 g per 30 g (1%).[<a href="#cit/section_4.180">

3364180</a>

3365]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3366<ul id="_451">

3367<li>

3368According to the Angiofibroma Grading Scale, patients who were treated with topical rapamycin showed a statistically and clinically significant improvement in facial angiofibromas.</li>

3369</ul>

3370</div>

3371</li>

3372</ol>

3373</div>

3374</section>

3375</section>

3376<section id="_65">

3377<h3 id="_65_toc">

3378Juvenile Nasopharyngeal Angiofibroma</h3>

3379<section id="_66">

3380<h4 id="_66_toc">

3381Clinical presentation and histopathology</h4>

3382<p id="_67" tabindex="-1">

3383Juvenile nasopharyngeal angiofibromas (JNA) account for 0.5% of all head and neck tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_4.181">

3384181</a>

3385] They typically occur in peri-pubertal males. While juvenile nasopharyngeal angiofibromas have not classically been included among vascular tumors, histologically, these tumors appear to be vascular tumors, with cells expressing vascular endothelial marker CD31, VEGFA, and VEGFR1. </p>

3386<p id="_452" tabindex="-1">

3387Despite their benign-appearing histology, juvenile nasopharyngeal angiofibromas can be locally destructive, spreading from the nasal cavity to the nasopharynx, paranasal sinuses, and orbit skull base, with intracranial extension. Some publications have suggested a hormonal influence on juvenile nasopharyngeal angiofibromas, with emphasis on the molecular mechanisms involved.[<a href="#cit/section_4.182">

3388182</a>

3389,<a href="#cit/section_4.183">

3390183</a>

3391] Nineteen patients with clinico-radiologically diagnosed primary juvenile nasopharyngeal angiofibromas underwent gallium Ga 68-[DOTA, 1-Nal3]-octreotide (68Ga-DOTANOC) positron emission tomography&#8211;computed tomography scans.[<a href="#cit/section_4.184">

3392184</a>

3393] The rationale for using this scan was the high expression of somatostatin receptors (SSTRs) in these tumors. DOTANOC expression was noted in all 19 cases of primary juvenile nasopharyngeal tumors (100%). The mean DOTANOC maximum standardized uptake value ratio of tumor and background was 6.9 (&#177;1.4) (range, 3.8&#8211;9.5). Intracranial extension in 13 of 19 patients was prominently visualized because of the absence of DOTANOC uptake in the brain. The authors suggested that these findings open up possibilities for physiological diagnostic imaging, with a promise of greater specificity and sensitivity. This scan may be applicable in ambivalent diagnostic situations, such as the detection of recurrence.</p>

3394</section>

3395<section id="_68">

3396<h4 id="_68_toc">

3397Treatment of juvenile nasopharyngeal angiofibroma</h4>

3398<p id="_69" tabindex="-1">

3399Surgical excision is the treatment of choice, but this can be challenging because of the extent of the lesion. A single-institution retrospective review of juvenile nasopharyngeal angiofibromas identified 37 patients with lateral extension.[<a href="#cit/section_4.185">

3400185</a>

3401] Anterior lateral extension to the pterygopalatine fossa occurred in 36 patients (97%) and further to the infratemporal fossa in 20 patients (54%). In 16 patients (43%), posterior lateral spread was observed (posterior to the pterygoid process and/or between its plates). The recurrence rate was 29.7% (11 of 37 patients). The recurrence rate in patients with anterior and/or posterior lateral extension was significantly higher than in patients with anterior lateral extension only. </p>

3402<p id="_200" tabindex="-1">

3403Juvenile nasopharyngeal angiofibromas have also been treated with radiation therapy, chemotherapy, alpha-interferon therapy, and sirolimus.[<a href="#cit/section_4.186">

3404186</a>

3405-<a href="#cit/section_4.190">

3406190</a>

3407]</p>

3408</section>

3409</section>

4140Liu Z, Wang J, Wang H, et al.: Hormonal receptors and vascular endothelial growth factor in juvenile nasopharyngeal angiofibroma: immunohistochemical and tissue microarray analysis. Acta Otolaryngol 135 (1): 51-7, 2015.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=25384380&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=25384380&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4141[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4142</li>

4143<li id="section_4.184">

4144Sakthivel P, Kumar R, Arunraj ST, et al.: 68 Ga DOTANOC PET/CT Scan in Primary Juvenile Nasopharyngeal Angiofibroma - A Pilot Study. Laryngoscope 131 (7): 1509-1515, 2021.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=33355921&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=33355921&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4145[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4146</li>

4147<li id="section_4.185">

4148Szyma&#324;ska A, Szyma&#324;ski M, Czekajska-Chehab E, et al.: Two types of lateral extension in juvenile nasopharyngeal angiofibroma: diagnostic and therapeutic management. Eur Arch Otorhinolaryngol 272 (1): 159-66, 2015.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=24599598&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=24599598&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4149[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4150</li>

Indian J Dermatol Venereol Leprol 81 (5): 540-1, 2015 Sep-Oct.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=26323682&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=26323682&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4153[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4154</li>

4155<li id="section_4.187">

Pediatrics 136 (3): e709-13, 2015.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=26304829&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=26304829&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4157[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4159<li id="section_4.188">

4161[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4162</li>

4165[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4216<section id="_71">

4217<h3 id="_71_toc">

4218Kaposiform Hemangioendothelioma and Tufted Angioma</h3>

4219<p id="_72" tabindex="-1">

4220Kaposiform hemangioendothelioma (KHE) and tufted angioma are rare vascular tumors that typically occur during infancy or early childhood but have been reported in adults. Both tumors are thought to be a spectrum of the same disease, because both can be locally aggressive and cause Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon, a serious life-threatening coagulopathy characterized by profound thrombocytopenia and hypofibrinogenemia. They are discussed here as a single entity, kaposiform hemangioendothelioma.</p>

4221<section id="_73">

4222<h4 id="_73_toc">

4223Incidence</h4>

4224<p id="_74" tabindex="-1">

4225The exact incidence of kaposiform hemangioendothelioma is unknown but is estimated to be 0.07 cases per 100,000 children per year.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

42261</a>

4227-<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

42283</a>

4229] This lesion affects both sexes equally, with most developing in the neonatal period, one-half presenting at birth, and others presenting during childhood or adulthood.[<a href="#cit/section_5.4">

42304</a>

4231]</p>

4232</section>

4233<section id="_78">

4234<h4 id="_78_toc">

4235Clinical presentation</h4>

4236<p id="_79" tabindex="-1">

4237Kaposiform hemangioendothelioma most frequently involves the extremities and less frequently involves the trunk and head and neck area.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

42383</a>

4239] Most lesions involve the skin (see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_173">

4240Figure 9</a>

4241). Deeper lesions (retroperitoneum, thoracic cavity, and muscle) can appear as a bluish-purpuric hue on the skin, whereas superficial lesions can be firm, purpuric or ecchymotic, and painful. Primary bone lesions may cause pain or other nonspecific findings, even without an obvious mass on physical examination.[<a href="#cit/section_5.5">

42425</a>

4243][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4244Level of evidence C2</a>

4245] Lesions are usually unifocal and growth is expansive and contiguous. Local lymph nodes may be involved, but there are no reports of distant metastasis. Rare multifocal presentations have been reported, mostly in the bone.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

42461</a>

4247-<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

42483</a>

4249]</p>

4250<p id="_172" tabindex="-1">

4251<figure id="figure_173" class="image-center">

4252<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/779673.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

4253Enlarge</a>

4254<img id="_173" alt="Photograph showing a Kaposiform hemangioendothelioma lesion on the right side of the face and neck." title="Photograph showing a Kaposiform hemangioendothelioma lesion on the right side of the face and neck." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/779673-750.jpg">

4255<figcaption class="caption-container">

4256Figure 9. Kaposiform hemangioendothelioma with Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon. The lesion is indurated, firm, and warm with petechiae and purpura. Credit: Denise Adams, M.D.</figcaption>

4257</figure>

4258</p>

4259<p id="_80" tabindex="-1">

4260Fifty to seventy percent of patients with kaposiform hemangioendothelioma develop Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon (KMP), which is a life-threatening complication. The risk of developing Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon is highest in patients with congenital lesions, lesions larger than 8 cm, and when kaposiform hemangioendothelioma arises in the retroperitoneum or mediastinum.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

42613</a>

4262,<a href="#cit/section_5.6">

42636</a>

4264] This condition is characterized by profound thrombocytopenia (range, 3,000/&#181;L&#8211;60,000/&#181;L) and hypofibrinogenemia (&lt;1 g/L). D-dimer and fibrin degradation products are elevated. Severe anemia can occur secondary to tumor sequestration. Severe hemorrhage is rare; however, trauma (biopsy, surgical procedure), ulceration, infection, or delay in initiating treatment may induce progression to disseminated intravascular coagulation, serious bleeding, and even death. Aggressive replacement of blood products, especially platelets, can increase the size of the lesion, causing significant pain and should only be considered with active bleeding and under the direction of a vascular anomalies specialist.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

42653</a>

4266] The mortality rate is unclear but it has been reported to be as high as 30%.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

42673</a>

4268,<a href="#cit/section_5.6">

42696</a>

4270]</p>

4271</section>

4272<section id="_75">

4273<h4 id="_75_toc">

4274Histopathology</h4>

4275<p id="_76" tabindex="-1">

4276Kaposiform hemangioendothelioma is characterized by sheets of spindle cells with an infiltrative pattern in the dermis, subcutaneous fat, and muscle. There are often areas of fibrosis, with dilated thin-walled vessels infiltrated around the areas of spindle cells. Mixed within these areas are nests of rounded epithelioid cells of vascular origin and aggregates of capillaries with round or irregularly shaped lumens containing platelet-rich fibrin thrombi. There are usually abnormal lymphatic spaces, either within or at the periphery of the lesion. The rate of mitosis is usually low but can be variable. Tufted angioma is characterized by multiple, discrete lobules of tightly packed capillaries (tufts) scattered in the dermis and sometimes in the subcutis, a so-called cannonball pattern.[<a href="#cit/section_5.7">

42777</a>

4278] Mitoses are rare.</p>

4279<p id="_77" tabindex="-1">

4280The pathogenesis is poorly understood. There is some evidence that kaposiform hemangioendothelioma may be derived from lymphatic endothelium, as the spindle cell expresses the vascular markers CD31 and CD34, the vascular endothelial growth factor receptor-3 (VEGFR-3) (a receptor required for lymphangiogenesis), and the lymphatic markers D2-40 and PROX1.[<a href="#cit/section_5.7">

42817</a>

4282-<a href="#cit/section_5.9">

42839</a>

4284] There is no evidence of association with human herpesvirus 8 infection as is present in Kaposi sarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_5.9">

42859</a>

4286] </p>

4287<p id="_453" tabindex="-1">

4288Genomic data are limited. There have been reports of a small number of patients with <em class="gene-name">

4289GNA14</em>

4290 mutations but not in all cases.[<a href="#cit/section_5.10">

429110</a>

4292,<a href="#cit/section_5.11">

429311</a>

4294]</p>

4295<p id="_413" tabindex="-1">

4296High serum levels of angiopoietin-2 (Ang-2) have been found in high-risk patients with kaposiform hemangioendothelioma and kaposiform lymphangiomatosis. The Ang-2 levels have also been noted to decrease in response to therapy with sirolimus, which raises the possibility of an effect on the endothelial cells of the kaposiform hemangioendothelioma tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_5.12">

429712</a>

4298] Ang-2 is produced and stored in the endothelial cells and acts as a TEK tyrosine kinase antagonist. Ang-2 can promote neovascularization in conjunction with VEGF, and in humans, Ang-2 is greatly increased in vascular remodeling that occurs with sepsis, inflammation, and lymphangiogenesis.[<a href="#cit/section_5.13">

429913</a>

4300] These levels have been used for the diagnosis of vascular tumors and assessment of response to therapy. </p>

4301</section>

4302<section id="_81">

4303<h4 id="_81_toc">

4304Diagnostic evaluation</h4>

4305<p id="_82" tabindex="-1">

4306The diagnosis is based on the combination of clinical, histological, and imaging features. Laboratory evaluation is essential for the diagnosis of Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon.

4307Whenever possible, histological confirmation should be obtained, because prolonged therapy is often needed. However, if clinical and imaging findings are highly suggestive of the diagnosis, deferring biopsy may be an option, but this decision should be reached via an interdisciplinary discussion and approach.</p>

4308<p id="_207" tabindex="-1">

4309

4310Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) is the preferred imaging modality, especially for kaposiform hemangioendothelioma with Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon and large lesions. T1-weighted sequences typically show a poorly circumscribed soft tissue mass with soft tissue and dermal thickening and diffuse enhancement with gadolinium. T2-weighted sequences show a diffuse increased signal, with stranding in the subcutaneous fat. Gradient sequences show mildly dilated vessels in and around the soft-tissue mass.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

43113</a>

4312]</p>

4313<p id="_414" tabindex="-1">

4314For small and superficial lesions, ultrasonography can be useful for diagnosis and can distinguish tufted angioma from kaposiform hemangioendothelioma. Tufted angiomas are more superficial, with well-defined borders and are hyperechoic. Kaposiform hemangioendothelioma has a more infiltrative pattern, with ill-defined borders and mixed echogenicity. Kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas also have an increased vascular density than do tufted angiomas.[<a href="#cit/section_5.14">

431514</a>

4316]</p>

4317</section>

4318<section id="_83">

4319<h4 id="_83_toc">

4320Treatment of kaposiform hemangioendothelioma and tufted angioma</h4>

4321<section id="_454">

4322<h5 id="_454_toc">

4323Treatment of uncomplicated kaposiform hemangioendothelioma and tufted angioma</h5>

4324<p id="_455" tabindex="-1">

4325There is no evidence-based standard of care for kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas and tufted angiomas. Treatment varies according to size, location, presence of symptoms, and severity of coagulopathy.</p>

4326<p id="_483" tabindex="-1">

4327Treatment options for uncomplicated kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas and tufted angiomas include the following:</p>

4328<div class="pdq-content-list">

4329<ol id="_484">

4330<li>

4331Observation.</li>

4332<li>

4333Surgical excision.</li>

4334<li>

4335Pulse-dye laser.</li>

4336<li>

4337Topical agents.</li>

4338<li>

4339Propranolol.</li>

4340<li>

4341Sirolimus with or without steroid therapy.</li>

4342</ol>

4343</div>

4344<p id="_485" tabindex="-1">

4345Observation is an option for patients with low-risk tumors (i.e., no Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon, small tumor size, asymptomatic). Spontaneous regression and/or stability has been noted.[<a href="#cit/section_5.15">

434615</a>

4347]</p>

4348<p id="_486" tabindex="-1">

4349 Kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas and tufted angiomas that are uncomplicated and localized can be treated with surgical excision, pulse-dye laser, or topical agents (steroids, sirolimus, or tacrolimus).[<a href="#cit/section_5.15">

435015</a>

4351-<a href="#cit/section_5.17">

435217</a>

4353]</p>

4354<p id="_268" tabindex="-1">

4355Propranolol therapy has been reported as a treatment option for patients with kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas on the basis of positive results of propranolol use for other more benign vascular tumors. Results have been mixed, with a report of improved effectiveness using higher doses of propranolol.[<a href="#cit/section_5.18">

435618</a>

4357,<a href="#cit/section_5.19">

435819</a>

4359] Preliminary results indicate that propranolol should be reserved for patients with kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas without Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon and with smaller, less complicated lesions.</p>

4360</section>

4361<section id="_456">

4362<h5 id="_456_toc">

4363Treatment of complicated kaposiform hemangioendothelioma and tufted angioma</h5>

4364<p id="_415" tabindex="-1">

4365Patients who have Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon and/or functional compromise and are symptomatic need aggressive therapy. An American and Canadian multidisciplinary expert panel published guidelines for the management of complicated kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_5.20">

436620</a>

4367] A number of treatment therapies have been reported but none have been uniformly effective.[<a href="#cit/section_5.21">

436821</a>

4369,<a href="#cit/section_5.22">

437022</a>

4371]</p>

4372<p id="_487" tabindex="-1">

4373Treatment options for complicated kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas and Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon include the following:</p>

4374<div class="pdq-content-list">

4375<ol id="_488">

4376<li>

4377<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_417">

4378Vincristine with or without steroid therapy</a>

4379.</li>

4380<li>

4381<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_418">

4382Sirolimus with or without steroid therapy</a>

4383.</li>

4384<li>

4385<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_489">

4386Surgical excision</a>

4387.</li>

4388</ol>

4389</div>

4390<section id="_417">

4391<h6 id="_417_toc">

4392Vincristine with or without steroid therapy</h6>

4393<p id="_85" tabindex="-1">

4394The most common treatment option for complicated kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas with or without Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon has traditionally been steroid therapy with or without vincristine or other agents.[<a href="#cit/section_5.20">

439520</a>

4396-<a href="#cit/section_5.25">

439725</a>

4398] However, many institutions are now using the mTOR inhibitor sirolimus, with or without steroid therapy, as primary treatment for high-risk patients.[<a href="#cit/section_5.26">

439926</a>

4400-<a href="#cit/section_5.30">

440130</a>

4402] Steroid therapy has not been effective as a single agent for complicated kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas, even at high doses. Patients treated with steroid therapy have a response rate of 10% to 20% and a significant number of side effects.[<a href="#cit/section_5.20">

440320</a>

4404] </p>

4405<p id="_87" tabindex="-1">

4406Vincristine was shown to have a hematologic response and reduction in tumor volume in patients with high-risk kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_5.21">

440721</a>

4408] Furthermore, in a retrospective review of 37 children with kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas whose lesions did not respond to steroids, 26 of the lesions achieved complete remission, with platelet counts reaching normal levels within 7.6&#8201;(&#177; 5.2) weeks after vincristine treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_5.23">

440923</a>

4410][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4411Level of evidence C3</a>

4412] Vincristine monotherapy in other studies has not been shown to be effective.[<a href="#cit/section_5.26">

441326</a>

4414,<a href="#cit/section_5.30">

441530</a>

4416] Successful management of patients with kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas who were treated with vincristine and ticlopidine has also been reported.[<a href="#cit/section_5.31">

441731</a>

4418]</p>

4419<p id="_459" tabindex="-1">

4420In 2013, consensus guidelines for the management of complicated kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas proposed the use of vincristine with or without steroids as first-line therapy. This recommendation was based on available evidence.[<a href="#cit/section_5.20">

442120</a>

4422]</p>

4423</section>

4424<section id="_418">

4425<h6 id="_418_toc">

4426Sirolimus with or without steroid therapy</h6>

4427<p id="_306" tabindex="-1">

4428Secondary to promising case reports, case series, and a prospective clinical trial, sirolimus may be considered an alternative first-line therapy for patients with kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_5.27">

442927</a>

4430,<a href="#cit/section_5.28">

443128</a>

4432,<a href="#cit/section_5.32">

443332</a>

4434] There are limited studies investigating the effect of sirolimus on kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas/tufted angiomas without Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon.</p>

4435<p id="_307" tabindex="-1">

4436Evidence (sirolimus therapy):</p>

4437<div class="pdq-content-list">

4438<ol id="_308">

4439<li>

4440In a prospective study that assessed the efficacy and safety of sirolimus for the treatment of complicated vascular anomalies, 13 patients with kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas were treated with sirolimus.[<a href="#cit/section_5.30">

444130</a>

4442] <div class="pdq-content-list">

4443<ul id="_309">

4444<li>

4445In patients with kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas and Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon, ten of ten patients had partial responses, with normalization of their platelet count and fibrinogen at the end of 6 and 12 courses.</li>

4446<li>

4447Of the three patients with kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas without Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon, two patients experienced partial responses by the end of course 12, and the third patient with multifocal bony disease had disease progression. </li>

4448<li>

4449Side effects were minimal in this group of young patients, and no patient with a kaposiform hemangioendothelioma required a dose adjustment or was removed from the study because of toxicity of sirolimus.</li>

4450</ul>

4451</div>

4452</li>

4453<li>

4454A retrospective study of sirolimus therapy in patients who had nearly all received previous other treatments reported a complete response rate of 73%. All patients assessed as having Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon had recovery of platelet counts between 1 day and 3 weeks (mean, 1.3 weeks).[<a href="#cit/section_5.33">

445533</a>

4456]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4457<ul id="_384">

4458<li>

4459One death occurred in this study from a respiratory infection in a child with a pleural effusion and multifocal disease involving the thoracic cavity. Biopsy was not routinely used in this study to confirm diagnosis, raising the possibility that this child had an unrecognized complex lymphatic anomaly.</li>

4460</ul>

4461</div>

4462</li>

4463<li>

4464A multicenter, retrospective cohort study analyzed 52 Chinese patients with progressive kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas. Thirty-seven patients (71%) had Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon. Those without Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon received sirolimus alone, and 21 of the patients with Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon received a combination of sirolimus and prednisone.[<a href="#cit/section_5.29">

446529</a>

4466]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4467<ul id="_342">

4468<li>

4469 Overall, 96% of patients at 6 months and 98% of patients at 12 months demonstrated improvement in notable symptoms and/or had improved complications.</li>

4470</ul>

4471</div>

4472</li>

4473<li>

4474A single case report of a child with a kaposiform hemangioendothelioma who developed recurrence of pain and fibrosis years after initial therapy and was treated with sirolimus for 26 months observed the following:[<a href="#cit/section_5.32">

447532</a>

4476]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4477<ul id="_310">

4478<li>

4479The patient's contracture and range of motion improved, the lesion shrank, and the child was well 2 years later.</li>

4480</ul>

4481</div>

4482 </li>

4483<li>

4484A prospective randomized study of patients with kaposiform hemangioendothelioma associated with Kasabach-Merritt syndrome compared sirolimus monotherapy with sirolimus in combination with prednisolone. The primary outcome was defined as achievement of a durable platelet response (platelet count &gt;100 &#215; 10<span class="sup">

44859</span>

4486/L) at week 4.[<a href="#cit/section_5.34">

448734</a>

4488][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810021&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810021&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4489Level of evidence A3</a>

4490]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4491<ul id="_2410">

4492<li>

4493At week 4, a durable platelet response was achieved in 35 of 37 patients who received sirolimus and prednisolone, compared with 24 of 36 patients who received sirolimus monotherapy (difference, 27.9%; 95% CI, 10.0%&#8211;44.7%).</li>

4494<li>

4495Compared with the sirolimus monotherapy group, the combination treatment group showed improvements in measures of durable platelet responses at all points during the initial 3-week treatment period. The durable platelet responses included median platelet counts during weeks 1 to 4, an increased number of patients achieving fibrinogen stabilization at week 4, and objective lesion responses at 12 months.</li>

4496<li>

4497 The frequencies of total and serious adverse events were similar in both groups.</li>

4498<li>

4499Patients who received combination therapy had lower total disease sequelae and fewer blood transfusions.</li>

4500</ul>

4501</div>

4502</li>

4503</ol>

4504</div>

4505<p id="_424" tabindex="-1">

4506Most high-risk patients (kaposiform hemangioendothelioma with Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon) are treated with sirolimus to achieve serum blood levels of 8 to 15 ng/mL.[<a href="#cit/section_5.29">

450729</a>

4508,<a href="#cit/section_5.30">

450930</a>

4510,<a href="#cit/section_5.35">

451135</a>

4512,<a href="#cit/section_5.36">

451336</a>

4514]</p>

4515<section id="_419">

4516<h6 id="_419_toc">

4517Supportive care and close monitoring of infants on sirolimus</h6>

4518<p id="_208" tabindex="-1">

4519A case report described two children with kaposiform hemangioendotheliomas and Kasabach-Merritt syndrome who died of pulmonary infections after treatment with sirolimus.[<a href="#cit/section_5.37">

452037</a>

4521] Another child who received sirolimus and prednisolone developed <em>

4522Pneumocystis jirovecii</em>

4523 pneumonia.[<a href="#cit/section_5.38">

452438</a>

4525] <em>

4526P. jirovecii</em>

4527 pneumonia prophylaxis and close monitoring of patients on sirolimus (especially infants) is encouraged. </p>

4528</section>

4529</section>

4530<section id="_489">

4531<h6 id="_489_toc">

4532Surgical excision</h6>

4533<p id="_416" tabindex="-1">

4534Surgical excision may be possible for lesions that did not respond to medical management or are life threatening. Embolization may be performed in conjunction with surgery or medical therapy; usually, it is a temporizing measure.[<a href="#cit/section_5.39">

453539</a>

4536]</p>

4537</section>

4538<section id="_421">

4539<h6 id="_421_toc">

4540Long-term outcomes</h6>

4541<p id="_88" tabindex="-1">

4542Even with therapy, these lesions do not fully regress and can recur. Worsened symptomatology (pain, inflammation) can occur with age, especially around the time of puberty.[<a href="#cit/section_5.40">

454340</a>

4544] </p>

4545<p id="_89" tabindex="-1">

4546Long-term effects include chronic pain, lymphedema, heart failure, and orthopedic issues.[<a href="#cit/section_5.39">

454739</a>

4548,<a href="#cit/section_5.40">

454940</a>

4550] These lesions prove to be a difficult dilemma for the practitioner because they have a varied clinical spectrum and response to therapy.</p>

4551</section>

4552</section>

4553<section id="_270">

4554<h5 id="_270_toc">

4555Treatment options under clinical evaluation for kaposiform hemangioendothelioma</h5>

4556<p id="_271" tabindex="-1">

4557Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

4558NCI website</a>

4559. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

4560ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

4561.</p>

4562</section>

4698[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4699</li>

4700<li id="section_5.34">

4701Ji Y, Chen S, Zhou J, et al.: Sirolimus plus prednisolone vs sirolimus monotherapy for kaposiform hemangioendothelioma: a randomized clinical trial. Blood 139 (11): 1619-1630, 2022.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=35030255&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=35030255&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4702[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4703</li>

4704<li id="section_5.35">

4705Zhang G, Chen H, Gao Y, et al.: Sirolimus for treatment of Kaposiform haemangioendothelioma with Kasabach-Merritt phenomenon: a retrospective cohort study. Br J Dermatol 178 (5): 1213-1214, 2018.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=29388191&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=29388191&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4706[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

Pediatr Blood Cancer 66 (8): e27810, 2019.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=31087627&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=31087627&amp;dopt=Abstract">

Pediatr Blood Cancer 64 (2): 284-286, 2017.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=27701822&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=27701822&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4726[PUBMED Abstract]</a>
