4859<section id="_319">

4860<h3 id="_319_toc">

4861Pseudomyogenic (Epithelioid Sarcoma&#8211;Like) Hemangioendothelioma</h3>

4862<section id="_324">

4863<h4 id="_324_toc">

4864Clinical presentation</h4>

4865<p id="_325" tabindex="-1">

4866Pseudomyogenic hemangioendotheliomas usually present in young men aged 20 to 50 years.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

48671</a>

4868,<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

48692</a>

4870] Multifocal disease occurs in 70% of patients and sites of involvement include the dermis, subcutis, and bones. Patients usually present with pain or a soft tissue mass.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

48711</a>

4872,<a href="#cit/section_6.3">

48733</a>

4874]</p>

4875</section>

4876<section id="_322">

4877<h4 id="_322_toc">

4878Histopathology and molecular features</h4>

4879<p id="_460" tabindex="-1">

4880Pseudomyogenic hemangioendotheliomas are rare, newly designated, distinct vascular tumors. They are characterized as intermediate-grade tumors with moderately aggressive local spread and rare distant metastatic disease.

4881

4882The etiology for this tumor is unclear, although a balanced translocation t(7;19) resulting in the <em class="gene-name">

4883SERPINE1</em>

4884::<em class="gene-name">

4885FOSB</em>

4886 fusion gene has been reported.[<a href="#cit/section_6.4">

48874</a>

4888]</p>

4889<p id="_323" tabindex="-1">

4890Pseudomyogenic hemangioendotheliomas are characterized by loose fascicles of plump spindle and epithelioid cells with abundant eosinophils, cytoplasm, and coexpression of keratins and endothelial markers.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

48911</a>

4892,<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

48932</a>

4894,<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

48955</a>

4896]</p>

4897</section>

4898<section id="_326">

4899<h4 id="_326_toc">

4900Treatment of pseudomyogenic hemangioendothelioma</h4>

4901<p id="_327" tabindex="-1">

4902Most patients with pseudomyogenic hemangioendotheliomas are treated with surgery, including amputation for multifocal bony disease.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

49031</a>

4904] In reported cases, chemotherapy has produced responses.[<a href="#cit/section_6.6">

49056</a>

4906,<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

49077</a>

4908] Recently, the mammalian target of rapamycin (mTOR) inhibitors have been considered as treatment options.[<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

49097</a>

4910,<a href="#cit/section_6.8">

49118</a>

4912] An additional case report noted efficacy of sirolimus with the addition of zoledronic acid in a patient with multifocal bony disease.[<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

49139</a>

4914] Tyrosine kinase inhibitors (pazopanib and telatinib) have also been used to successfully treat pseudomyogenic hemangioendothelioma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

491510</a>

4916,<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

491711</a>

4918]</p>

4919</section>

4920</section>

4921<section id="_91">

4922<h3 id="_91_toc">

4923Retiform Hemangioendothelioma</h3>

4924<section id="_92">

4925<h4 id="_92_toc">

4926Clinical presentation</h4>

4927<p id="_461" tabindex="-1">

4928Retiform hemangioendotheliomas are slow growing, exophytic, flat tumors found in young adults and occasionally children.[<a href="#cit/section_6.12">

492912</a>

4930] They are usually located in the limbs and trunk. Local recurrences are common, but distinct metastases are extremely rare.[<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

493113</a>

4932]</p>

4933</section>

4934<section id="_490">

4935<h4 id="_490_toc">

4936Histopathology</h4>

4937<p id="_93" tabindex="-1">

4938Histologically, retiform hemangioendotheliomas are located in the dermis and subcutaneous tissue. Vessels exhibit a pattern resembling the rete testis and are lined by protruding endothelial cells. They do not express lymphatic markers but stain positive for endothelial markers.[<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

493913</a>

4940]</p>

4941</section>

4942<section id="_96">

4943<h4 id="_96_toc">

4944Treatment of retiform hemangioendothelioma</h4>

4945<p id="_97" tabindex="-1">

4946Treatment for patients with retiform hemangioendotheliomas includes surgical excision with adequate tumor margins and monitoring for local recurrence. There are case reports describing the use of radiation therapy and chemotherapy for inoperable and recurrent tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_6.14">

494714</a>

4948-<a href="#cit/section_6.17">

494917</a>

4950]</p>

4951</section>

4952</section>

4953<section id="_98">

4954<h3 id="_98_toc">

4955Papillary Intralymphatic Angioendothelioma</h3>

4956<section id="_99">

4957<h4 id="_99_toc">

4958Clinical presentation</h4>

4959<p id="_100" tabindex="-1">

4960Papillary intralymphatic angioendotheliomas, also known as Dabska tumors, can occur in the adult and pediatric population.[<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

496118</a>

4962] The lesions occur in the dermis and subcutis on all body parts and there have been some reports of lymph node involvement. They can be large or small raised purplish firm nodules.</p>

4963</section>

4964<section id="_491">

4965<h4 id="_491_toc">

4966Histopathology</h4>

4967<p id="_101" tabindex="-1">

4968Pathologically, papillary intralymphatic angioendothelioma lesions reveal intravascular growth of well-differentiated endothelial cells in a columnar configuration. They have thickened hyaline walls with hobnailed endothelium. Vascular endothelial growth factor receptor type 3, a marker for lymphatic endothelium, is positive in most cases. There is minimal cytological atypia.[<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

496919</a>

4970] Some lesions are associated with vascular malformations.</p>

4971</section>

4972<section id="_102">

4973<h4 id="_102_toc">

4974Treatment of papillary intralymphatic angioendothelioma</h4>

4975<p id="_103" tabindex="-1">

4976Surgical excision is the treatment of choice for patients with papillary intralymphatic angioendotheliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

497720</a>

4978]</p>

4979</section>

4980</section>

4981<section id="_146">

4982<h3 id="_146_toc">

4983Composite Hemangioendothelioma</h3>

4984<section id="_147">

4985<h4 id="_147_toc">

4986Clinical presentation</h4>

4987<p id="_210" tabindex="-1">

4988Composite hemangioendotheliomas usually occur in the dermis and subcutis of the distal extremities but has been found in other areas such as the head, neck, and mediastinum.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

498921</a>

4990] They have been reported in all age groups.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

499121</a>

4992] </p>

4993<p id="_462" tabindex="-1">

4994Composite hemangioendotheliomas recur locally and rarely metastasize.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

499521</a>

4996,<a href="#cit/section_6.22">

499722</a>

4998] Regional lymph nodes are the most likely site of metastasis and require imaging evaluation for surveillance.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

499921</a>

5000]</p>

5001</section>

5002<section id="_492">

5003<h4 id="_492_toc">

5004Histopathology</h4>

5005<p id="_148" tabindex="-1">

5006Composite hemangioendotheliomas are very rare vascular tumors classified as intermediate because of the combined benign and malignant vascular components. Usually, combined epithelioid and retiform variants are noted but some tumors have three components (epithelioid, retiform, and spindle cell).[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

500721</a>

5008] Angiosarcoma foci have been noted. Pathology reveals positivity for CD31, factor VIII, and vimentin.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

500921</a>

5010,<a href="#cit/section_6.22">

501122</a>

5012] Rarely, D-240 is positive with a Ki-67 index of approximately 20%.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

501321</a>

5014]</p>

5015</section>

5016<section id="_151">

5017<h4 id="_151_toc">

5018Treatment of composite hemangioendothelioma</h4>

5019<p id="_152" tabindex="-1">

5020Surgical removal is the treatment of choice for patients with composite hemangioendotheliomas, although radiation therapy and chemotherapy have been used for metastatic disease.[<a href="#cit/section_6.23">

502123</a>

5022,<a href="#cit/section_6.24">

502324</a>

5024]</p>

5025</section>

5026</section>

5027<section id="_153">

5028<h3 id="_153_toc">

5029Kaposi Sarcoma</h3>

5030<section id="_154">

5031<h4 id="_154_toc">

5032Clinical presentation</h4>

5033<p id="_155" tabindex="-1">

5034Kaposi sarcoma (KS) is a rare malignant vascular tumor associated with a viral etiology (human herpesvirus 8).[<a href="#cit/section_6.25">

503525</a>

5036] The skin lesions were first described in 1872 by Moritz Kaposi. The incidence has increased worldwide as a result of the HIV-AIDS epidemic. It is an extremely rare diagnosis in children. Epidemic and iatrogenic forms of Kaposi sarcoma in children result from profound acquired T-cell deficiency that is caused by HIV infections, rare immune disorders, or solid organ transplants.</p>

5037<p id="_156" tabindex="-1">

5038A retrospective study has investigated the presentation of Kaposi sarcoma in children in endemic areas of Africa. Children usually present with cutaneous lesions, lymphadenopathy, and intrathoracic and oral lesions. Cutaneous lesions initially appear as red, purple, or brown macules, later developing into plaques and then nodules.[<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

503926</a>

5040-<a href="#cit/section_6.28">

504128</a>

5042]</p>

5043</section>

5044<section id="_157">

5045<h4 id="_157_toc">

5046Treatment of Kaposi sarcoma</h4>

5047<p id="_158" tabindex="-1">

5048Children with Kaposi sarcoma have responded to treatment with chemotherapy regimens, including bleomycin, vincristine, and taxanes, although there are no prospective clinical trials. Because Kaposi sarcoma is rare in the pediatric population, there are few evidence-based studies.</p>

5049<p id="_463" tabindex="-1">

5050Evidence (chemotherapy):</p>

5051<div class="pdq-content-list">

5052<ol id="_464">

5053<li>

5054Fifty-six Malawian children aged 3 to 12 years with Kaposi sarcoma were treated with six courses of vincristine, bleomycin, and oral etoposide. This was a high-risk population because 48 of the patients (86%) were HIV positive, 36 of whom (77%) were on antiretroviral therapy (ART).[<a href="#cit/section_6.29">

505529</a>

5056][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5057Level of evidence C1</a>

5058]<div class="pdq-content-list">

5059<ul id="_465">

5060<li>

5061Eighteen patients (32%) had a complete remission.</li>

5062<li>

5063 At 12 months, the overall survival (OS) rate was 71%, and the event-free survival rate was 50%.</li>

5064<li>

5065Quality of life improved in 45 patients (80%).</li>

5066</ul>

5067</div>

5068</li>

5069</ol>

5070</div>

5071<p id="_2411" tabindex="-1">

5072In one retrospective series, 207 children and adolescents with endemic or HIV-related Kaposi sarcoma were treated with unspecified protocols and ART between 2006 and 2015. The study reported a 7-year OS rate of 37% (76 patients). Of these patients, 62% had complete responses, and 8% had stable partial responses. Four of the patients with complete responses had been treated with ART without chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.30">

507330</a>

5074][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5075Level of evidence C1</a>

5076]

5077</p>

5078<p id="_466" tabindex="-1">

5079Even in adults, the evidence and quality of studies are poor, and it is difficult to recommend particular treatment regimens. Other treatment options have been based on adult studies (refer directly below).</p>

5080<p id="_314" tabindex="-1">

5081In a systematic review of treatment for classic Kaposi sarcoma, 26 articles published from 1980 to 2010 were reviewed. Articles describing populations at high risk secondary to previous transplantation and endemic and epidemic Kaposi sarcoma were excluded.[<a href="#cit/section_6.31">

508231</a>

5083] All articles had a minimum of five patients per intervention. A greater than 50% decrease in the size of the lesions or lymphedema was considered a response. The quality of the articles was considered poor, primarily because of lack of uniform staging criteria and variable means of assessing response. The following response rates for systemic treatments were noted:</p>

5084<div class="pdq-content-list">

5085<ul id="_311">

5086<li>

5087 Pegylated doxorubicin: 71% to 100%.</li>

5088<li>

5089Vinca alkaloids: 58% to 90%.</li>

5090<li>

5091 Etoposide: 74% to 76%.</li>

5092<li>

5093 Taxanes: 93% to 100%.</li>

5094<li>

5095 Gemcitabine: 100%.</li>

5096<li>

5097Vinblastine and bleomycin: 97%.</li>

5098<li>

5099Interferon alfa-2: 71% to 100%.</li>

5100</ul>

5101</div>

5102<p id="_312" tabindex="-1">

5103For local therapies, the following response rates were reported: </p>

5104<div class="pdq-content-list">

5105<ul id="_313">

5106<li>

5107 Intralesional vincristine: 62%.</li>

5108<li>

5109 Intralesional interferon alfa-2: 50% to 90%.</li>

5110<li>

5111 Imiquimod: 56%.</li>

5112<li>

5113 Radiation therapy: 63% to 93%.[<a href="#cit/section_6.32">

511432</a>

5115-<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

511634</a>

5117]</li>

5118</ul>

5119</div>

5120<p id="_289" tabindex="-1">

5121For more information about the treatment of Kaposi sarcoma in adults, see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/kaposi-treatment-pdq">

5122Kaposi Sarcoma Treatment</a>

5123.</p>

5124</section>

5346<section id="_191">

5347<h3 id="_191_toc">

5348Epithelioid Hemangioendothelioma</h3>

5349<section id="_sm_CDR0000779194_105">

5350<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779194_105_toc">

5351Incidence and outcome</h4>

5352<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_108" tabindex="-1">

5353Epithelioid hemangioendothelioma was first described in soft tissue by Weiss and Enzinger in 1982. These tumors can occur in younger patients, but the peak incidence is in the fourth and fifth decades of life. The number of pediatric patients reported in the literature is limited. </p>

5354<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_151" tabindex="-1">

5355Epithelioid hemangioendotheliomas can have an indolent or very aggressive course, with an overall survival rate of 73% at 5 years. There are case reports of patients with untreated multiple lesions who have a very benign course. However, other patients have a very aggressive course. Some pathologists have tried to stratify patients to evaluate risks and adjust treatment, but more research is needed.[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

53561</a>

5357-<a href="#cit/section_7.7">

53587</a>

5359]</p>

5360<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_143" tabindex="-1">

5361A multi-institutional case series reported on 24 patients aged 2 to 26 years with epithelioid hemangioendotheliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_7.8">

53628</a>

5363][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5364Level of evidence C2</a>

5365] Most patients presented with multiorgan disease. Progression was seen in 63% of patients, with a mean time to progression of 18.4 months (range, 0&#8211;72 months).</p>

5366<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_124" tabindex="-1">

5367The presence of effusions, tumor size larger than 3 cm, and a high mitotic index (&gt;3 mitoses/50 high-power fields) have been associated with unfavorable outcomes.[<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

53683</a>

5369]</p>

5370</section>

5371<section id="_sm_CDR0000779194_106">

5372<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779194_106_toc">

5373Clinical presentation and diagnostic evaluation</h4>

5374<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_107" tabindex="-1">

5375Common sites of involvement are liver alone (21%), liver plus lung (18%), lung alone (12%), and bone alone (14%).[<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

53763</a>

5377,<a href="#cit/section_7.9">

53789</a>

5379,<a href="#cit/section_7.10">

538010</a>

5381] Clinical presentation depends on the site of involvement, as follows: </p>

5382<div class="pdq-content-list">

5383<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779194_113">

5384<li>

5385<strong>

5386Liver:</strong>

5387 Hepatic nodules have central vascularity on ultrasound, contrast-enhancing lesions by computed tomography, and low T1 signal and moderate T2 signal on magnetic resonance imaging. These may be incidental findings in asymptomatic patients, but most patients commonly present with signs or symptoms of cholestasis, including pruritus, jaundice, or scleral icterus.</li>

5388<li>

5389<strong>

5390Lung:</strong>

5391 Pulmonary epithelioid hemangioendothelioma may be an asymptomatic finding on chest x-ray or be associated with pleuritic pain, hemoptysis, anemia, and fibrosis.</li>

5392<li>

5393<strong>

5394Bone:</strong>

5395 Bone metastasis may be associated with pathological fracture. On x-rays, they are well-defined osteolytic lesions and can be multiple or solitary.</li>

5396<li>

5397<strong>

5398Soft tissue:</strong>

5399 Thirty percent of soft tissue cases are associated with metastases. When present, metastatic disease can be very aggressive and have a limited response to chemotherapy.</li>

5400<li>

5401<strong>

5402Skin:</strong>

5403 Cutaneous lesions can be raised and nodular or can be warm, red-brown plaques.</li>

5404</ul>

5405</div>

5406</section>

5407<section id="_sm_CDR0000779194_109">

5408<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779194_109_toc">

5409Genomic alterations and histopathological features</h4>

5410<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_110" tabindex="-1">

5411<em class="gene-name">

5412WWTR1</em>

5413::<em class="gene-name">

5414CAMTA1</em>

5415 gene fusions have been found in most patients. Less commonly, <em class="gene-name">

5416YAP1</em>

5417::<em class="gene-name">

5418TFE3</em>

5419 gene fusions have been reported.[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

54201</a>

5421] These gene fusions are not directly targetable with current medications. Monoclonality has been described in multiple liver lesions, suggesting a metastatic process.</p>

5422<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_119" tabindex="-1">

5423Histologically, these lesions are characterized as epithelioid lesions arranged in nests, strands, and trabecular patterns, with infrequent vascular spaces. Features that may be associated with aggressive clinical behavior include cellular atypia, one or more mitoses per 10 high-power fields, an increased proportion of spindled cells, focal necrosis, and metaplastic bone formation.[<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

54243</a>

5425]</p>

5426</section>

5427<section id="_sm_CDR0000779194_111">

5428<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779194_111_toc">

5429Treatment of epithelioid hemangioendothelioma</h4>

5430<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_115" tabindex="-1">

5431Treatment options for epithelioid hemangioendothelioma include the following:</p>

5432<div class="pdq-content-list">

5433<ol id="_sm_CDR0000779194_116">

5434<li>

5435Observation.</li>

5436<li>

5437Surgery.</li>

5438<li>

5439Immunotherapy.</li>

5440<li>

5441Targeted therapy.</li>

5442<li>

5443Chemotherapy.</li>

5444<li>

5445Radiation therapy.</li>

5446</ol>

5447</div>

5448<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_112" tabindex="-1">

5449For indolent cases, observation is warranted. Surgery is performed when resection is possible. Liver transplant has been used with aggressive liver lesions, both with and without metastases.[<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

54503</a>

5451,<a href="#cit/section_7.11">

545211</a>

5453-<a href="#cit/section_7.13">

545413</a>

5455]</p>

5456<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_148" tabindex="-1">

5457For more aggressive cases, several different drugs have been used, including interferon, thalidomide, sorafenib, pazopanib, and sirolimus.[<a href="#cit/section_7.11">

545811</a>

5459,<a href="#cit/section_7.14">

546014</a>

5461,<a href="#cit/section_7.15">

546215</a>

5463] The most aggressive cases are treated with angiosarcoma-type chemotherapy. </p>

5464<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_144" tabindex="-1">

5465A multi-institutional case series reported on 24 patients aged 2 to 26 years with epithelioid hemangioendothelioma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.8">

54668</a>

5467][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5468Level of evidence C2</a>

5469]</p>

5470<div class="pdq-content-list">

5471<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779194_152">

5472<li>

5473 Three patients who were treated with sirolimus achieved stable disease or a partial response for more than 2.5 years.</li>

5474</ul>

5475</div>

5476<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_153" tabindex="-1">

5477 A report from 2020 that investigated sirolimus treatment in children aimed to add to the previous experience of sirolimus in adults. A retrospective review identified six pediatric patients with disseminated epithelioid hemangioendothelioma who were treated with sirolimus.[<a href="#cit/section_7.16">

547816</a>

5479] </p>

5480<div class="pdq-content-list">

5481<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779194_154">

5482<li>

5483Four of the six patients demonstrated partial responses or disease stabilization.</li>

5484</ul>

5485</div>

5486<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_155" tabindex="-1">

5487A report from the European Soft Tissue Sarcoma study group analyzed ten patients with localized disease and one patient with metastatic disease from two studies.[<a href="#cit/section_7.17">

548817</a>

5489] The median age was 14.3 years (range, 9.0&#8211;18.8 years). Local therapy was initial primary surgery in seven patients, and five patients received systemic therapy. No patients received radiation therapy. </p>

5490<div class="pdq-content-list">

5491<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779194_156">

5492<li>

5493After a median follow-up of 50 months (range, 6&#8211;176 months) for living patients, nine patients remained alive and off therapy and two patients died. </li>

5494<li>

5495The 5-year progression-free survival rate was 77.1% (95% confidence interval [CI], 34.5%&#8211;93.9%).</li>

5496<li>

5497The 5-year overall survival rate was 74.1% (95% CI, 28.1%&#8211;93.0%).</li>

5498</ul>

5499</div>

5500<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_139" tabindex="-1">

5501Patients or families who desire additional disease-directed therapy should consider entering trials of novel therapeutic approaches because no standard agents have demonstrated clinically significant activity.</p>

5502<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_140" tabindex="-1">

5503Regardless of whether a decision is made to pursue disease-directed therapy at the time of progression, palliative care remains a central focus of management. This ensures that quality of life is maximized while attempting to reduce symptoms and stress related to the terminal illness.</p>

5504</section>

5505<section id="_sm_CDR0000779194_120">

5506<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779194_120_toc">

5507Treatment options under clinical evaluation for epithelioid hemangioendothelioma</h4>

5508<p id="_sm_CDR0000779194_121" tabindex="-1">

5509Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

5510NCI website</a>

5511. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

5512ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

5513.</p>

5514<section id="_TrialSearch_sm_CDR0000779194_120_sid_7">

5515<h5 id="_TrialSearch_sm_CDR0000779194_120_sid_7_toc">

5516Current Clinical Trials</h5>

5517<p id="_TrialSearch_sm_CDR0000779194_120_22" tabindex="-1">

5518Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

5519advanced clinical trial search</a>

5520 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

5521General information</a>

5522 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

5523</section>

5524</section>

5525</section>

5526<section id="_192">

5527<h3 id="_192_toc">

5528Angiosarcoma</h3>

5529<section id="_sm_CDR0000779195_114">

5530<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779195_114_toc">

5531Incidence and clinical presentation</h4>

5532<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_115" tabindex="-1">

5533Angiosarcomas are rare (accounting for 2% of sarcomas), aggressive, vascular tumors that can arise in any part of the body but is more common in soft tissues. Angiosarcoma has an estimated incidence of 2 cases per 1 million people. In the United States, it affects approximately 600 people annually, who are typically aged 60 to 70 years.[<a href="#cit/section_7.18">

553418</a>

5535] </p>

5536<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_165" tabindex="-1">

5537Angiosarcomas are extremely rare in children. It is unclear if the pathophysiology of angiosarcomas in children differs from that of angiosarcomas in adults. Cases have been reported in neonates and toddlers, with presentation of multiple cutaneous lesions and liver lesions, some of which are GLUT1 positive.[<a href="#cit/section_7.19">

553819</a>

5539-<a href="#cit/section_7.22">

554022</a>

5541] Most angiosarcomas involve the skin and superficial soft tissue, although the liver, spleen, and lung can be affected; bone is rarely affected.</p>

5542<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_208" tabindex="-1">

5543Nomenclature of these liver lesions has been difficult and confusing with use of outdated terminology proposed in 1971 (e.g., type I hemangioendothelioma: infantile hemangioma; type II hemangioendothelioma: low-grade angiosarcoma; type III hemangioendothelioma: high-grade angiosarcoma).[<a href="#cit/section_7.20">

554420</a>

5545] A report of eight cases of liver angiosarcomas in children highlighted the misuse of the term hemangioendothelioma and the importance of early diagnosis and treatment of these tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_7.23">

554623</a>

5547]</p>

5548</section>

5549<section id="_sm_CDR0000779195_116">

5550<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779195_116_toc">

5551Risk factors</h4>

5552<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_117" tabindex="-1">

5553Established risk factors include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_7.24">

555424</a>

5555]</p>

5556<div class="pdq-content-list">

5557<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779195_193">

5558<li>

5559 Vinyl chloride exposure.</li>

5560<li>

5561 Radiation exposure.</li>

5562<li>

5563Chronic lymphedema from any cause, including Stewart-Treves syndrome.</li>

5564</ul>

5565</div>

5566</section>

5567<section id="_sm_CDR0000779195_118">

5568<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779195_118_toc">

5569Genomic alterations and histopathological features</h4>

5570<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_119" tabindex="-1">

5571Angiosarcomas are largely aneuploid tumors. The rare cases of angiosarcoma that arise from benign lesions such as hemangiomas have a distinct pathway that needs to be investigated. <em class="gene-name">

5572MYC</em>

5573 amplification is seen in radiation-induced angiosarcoma. <em class="gene-name">

5574KDR</em>

5575 mutations and <em class="gene-name">

5576FLT4</em>

5577 amplifications have been seen with a frequency of less than 50%.[<a href="#cit/section_7.24">

557824</a>

5579]</p>

5580<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_120" tabindex="-1">

5581Histopathological diagnosis can be very difficult because there can be areas of varied atypia. A common feature of angiosarcoma is an irregular network of channels in a dissective pattern along dermal collagen bundles. There is varied cellular shape, size, mitosis, endothelial multilayering, and papillary formation. Epithelioid cells can also be present. Necrosis and hemorrhage are common. Tumors stain for factor VIII, CD31, and CD34. Some liver lesions can mimic infantile hemangiomas and have focal GLUT1 positivity.[<a href="#cit/section_7.20">

558220</a>

5583]</p>

5584</section>

5585<section id="_sm_CDR0000779195_122">

5586<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779195_122_toc">

5587Treatment of angiosarcoma</h4>

5588<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_167" tabindex="-1">

5589Treatment options for angiosarcoma include the following:</p>

5590<div class="pdq-content-list">

5591<ol id="_sm_CDR0000779195_173">

5592<li>

5593<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_sm_CDR0000779195_209">

5594Surgery (localized disease)</a>

5595.</li>

5596<li>

5597<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_sm_CDR0000779195_214">

5598Radiation therapy (localized cutaneous disease in adults)</a>

5599.</li>

5600<li>

5601<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_sm_CDR0000779195_216">

5602Surgery, chemotherapy, and radiation therapy (metastatic disease)</a>

5603.</li>

5604<li>

5605<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq#_sm_CDR0000779195_221">

5606Palliative care</a>

5607.</li>

5608</ol>

5609</div>

5610<section id="_sm_CDR0000779195_209">

5611<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779195_209_toc">

5612Surgery</h5>

5613<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_210" tabindex="-1">

5614Localized disease can be cured by aggressive surgery. Complete surgical excision

5615appears to be crucial for the long-term survival of patients with angiosarcomas and lymphangiosarcomas, despite evidence

5616of tumor shrinkage in some patients who were treated with local or systemic therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.21">

561721</a>

5618,<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

561925</a>

5620-<a href="#cit/section_7.27">

562127</a>

5622] Data on liver transplant for localized angiosarcomas are limited.[<a href="#cit/section_7.28">

562328</a>

5624][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5625Level of evidence C1</a>

5626] </p>

5627<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_211" tabindex="-1">

5628Evidence (surgery):</p>

5629<div class="pdq-content-list">

5630<ol id="_sm_CDR0000779195_212">

5631<li>

5632 A review of 222 patients (median age, 62 years; range, 15&#8211;90 years) reported the following:[<a href="#cit/section_7.27">

563327</a>

5634] <div class="pdq-content-list">

5635<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779195_213">

5636<li>

5637An overall disease-specific survival (DSS) rate of 38% at 5 years. </li>

5638<li>

5639The 5-year DSS rate was 44% in 138 patients with localized, resected tumors but only 16% in 43 patients with metastases at diagnosis.</li>

5640</ul>

5641</div>

5642</li>

5643<li>

5644One case report suggested that liver transplant may contribute to prolonged disease-free survival.[<a href="#cit/section_7.29">

564529</a>

5646][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5647Level of evidence C2</a>

5648]</li>

5649</ol>

5650</div>

5651</section>

5652<section id="_sm_CDR0000779195_214">

5653<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779195_214_toc">

5654Radiation therapy</h5>

5655<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_215" tabindex="-1">

5656Localized disease, especially cutaneous angiosarcomas, can be treated with radiation therapy or combined chemotherapy (e.g., paclitaxel) and radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.30">

565730</a>

5658] Most of these reported cases are in adults.[<a href="#cit/section_7.31">

565931</a>

5660] When radiation is used, the doses are high (50&#8211;70 Gy), the cutaneous volumes are extensive because of the infiltrating nature of the disease, and regional (draining) nodes are often included, even if clinically negative.[<a href="#cit/section_7.32">

566132</a>

5662,<a href="#cit/section_7.33">

566333</a>

5664] Because of these factors, radiation therapy is rarely used to treat children.</p>

5665</section>

5666<section id="_sm_CDR0000779195_216">

5667<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779195_216_toc">

5668Surgery, chemotherapy, and radiation therapy</h5>

5669<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_217" tabindex="-1">

5670Multimodal treatment with surgery, systemic chemotherapy, and radiation therapy is used for metastatic disease, although it is rarely curative.[<a href="#cit/section_7.33">

567133</a>

5672,<a href="#cit/section_7.34">

567334</a>

5674] Disease control is the objective in patients with metastatic angiosarcomas. Published progression-free survival is between 3 months and 7 months,[<a href="#cit/section_7.35">

567535</a>

5676] and the median overall survival (OS) is 14 to 18 months.[<a href="#cit/section_7.36">

567736</a>

5678] In both adults and children, the 5-year OS rates are between 20% and 35%.[<a href="#cit/section_7.21">

567921</a>

5680,<a href="#cit/section_7.22">

568122</a>

5682,<a href="#cit/section_7.37">

568337</a>

5684]

5685</p>

5686<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_218" tabindex="-1">

5687One child who was diagnosed with angiosarcoma secondary to malignant transformation from infantile hemangioma responded to treatment with bevacizumab (a monoclonal antibody against vascular endothelial growth factor) combined with systemic chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.19">

568819</a>

5689,<a href="#cit/section_7.34">

569034</a>

5691]</p>

5692<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_219" tabindex="-1">

5693Biologic agents that inhibit angiogenesis have shown activity in adults with angiosarcomas.[<a href="#cit/section_7.20">

569420</a>

5695,<a href="#cit/section_7.37">

569637</a>

5697]</p>

5698<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_220" tabindex="-1">

5699There is one case report of a pediatric patient with metastatic cardiac angiosarcoma who was successfully treated with conventional chemotherapy, radiation, surgery, and targeted therapies, including pazopanib.[<a href="#cit/section_7.38">

570038</a>

5701]</p>

5702</section>

5703<section id="_sm_CDR0000779195_221">

5704<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779195_221_toc">

5705Palliative care</h5>

5706<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_222" tabindex="-1">

5707Regardless of whether a decision is made to pursue disease-directed therapy at the time of progression, palliative care remains a central focus of management. This ensures that quality of life is maximized while attempting to reduce symptoms and stress related to the terminal illness.</p>

5708</section>

5709</section>

5710<section id="_sm_CDR0000779195_170">

5711<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779195_170_toc">

5712Treatment options under clinical evaluation for angiosarcoma</h4>

5713<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_223" tabindex="-1">

5714Patients or families who desire additional disease-directed therapy should consider entering trials of novel therapeutic approaches because no standard agents have demonstrated clinically significant activity.</p>

5715<p id="_sm_CDR0000779195_171" tabindex="-1">

5716Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

5717NCI website</a>

5718. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

5719ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

5720.</p>

5926 <section id="_AboutThis_1">

5927

5928 <h2>

5929About This PDQ Summary</h2>

5930

5931 <div id="_section_AboutThis_1" class="pdq-sections">

5932<section id="_AboutThis_2">

5933<h3 id="_AboutThis_2_toc">

5934Purpose of This Summary</h3>

5935<p id="_AboutThis_3" tabindex="-1">

5936This PDQ cancer information summary for health professionals provides comprehensive, peer-reviewed, evidence-based information about the treatment of childhood vascular tumors. It is intended as a resource to inform and assist clinicians in the care of their patients. It does not provide formal guidelines or recommendations for making health care decisions.</p>

5937</section>

5938<section id="_AboutThis_4">

5939<h3 id="_AboutThis_4_toc">

5940Reviewers and Updates</h3>

5941<p id="_AboutThis_5" tabindex="-1">

5942This summary is reviewed regularly and updated as necessary by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment">

5943PDQ Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board</a>

5944, which is editorially independent of the National Cancer Institute (NCI). The summary reflects an independent review of the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or the National Institutes of Health (NIH).</p>

5945<p id="_AboutThis_22" tabindex="-1">

5946 Board members review recently published articles each month to determine whether an article should:</p>

5947<div class="pdq-content-list">

5948<ul id="_AboutThis_6">

5949<li>

5950be discussed at a meeting,</li>

5951<li>

5952be cited with text, or</li>

5953<li>

5954replace or update an existing article that is already cited.</li>

5955</ul>

5956</div>

5957<p id="_AboutThis_7" tabindex="-1">

5958Changes to the summaries are made through a consensus process in which Board members evaluate the strength of the evidence in the published articles and determine how the article should be included in the summary.</p>

5959<p id="" tabindex="-1">

5960The lead reviewers for Childhood Vascular Tumors Treatment are:</p>

5961<div class="pdq-content-list">

5962<ul id="">

5963<li>

5964Denise Adams, MD (Children's Hospital Boston)</li>

5965<li>

5966Sally J. Cohen-Cutler, MD, MS (Children's Hospital of Philadelphia)</li>

5967<li>

5968Louis S. Constine, MD (James P. Wilmot Cancer Center at University of Rochester Medical Center)</li>

5969<li>

5970Holcombe Edwin Grier, MD</li>

5971<li>

5972Paul A. Meyers, MD (Memorial Sloan-Kettering Cancer Center)</li>

5973<li>

5974Thomas A. Olson, MD (Aflac Cancer and Blood Disorders Center of Children's Healthcare of Atlanta - Egleston Campus)</li>

5975<li>

5976Alberto S. Pappo, MD (St. Jude Children's Research Hospital)</li>

5977<li>

5978Stephen J. Shochat, MD (St. Jude Children's Research Hospital)</li>

5979<li>

5980Malcolm A. Smith, MD, PhD (National Cancer Institute)</li>

5981</ul>

5982</div>

5983<p id="_AboutThis_9" tabindex="-1">

5984Any comments or questions about the summary content should be submitted to Cancer.gov through the NCI website's <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us">

5985Email Us</a>

5986. Do not contact the individual Board Members with questions or comments about the summaries. Board members will not respond to individual inquiries.</p>

5987</section>

5988<section id="_AboutThis_10">

5989<h3 id="_AboutThis_10_toc">

5990Levels of Evidence</h3>

5991<p id="_AboutThis_11" tabindex="-1">

5992Some of the reference citations in this summary are accompanied by a level-of-evidence designation. These designations are intended to help readers assess the strength of the evidence supporting the use of specific interventions or approaches. The PDQ Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board uses a <a href="/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/treatment">

5993formal evidence ranking system</a>

5994 in developing its level-of-evidence designations.</p>

5995</section>

5996<section id="_AboutThis_12">

5997<h3 id="_AboutThis_12_toc">

5998Permission to Use This Summary</h3>

5999<p id="_AboutThis_13" tabindex="-1">

6000PDQ is a registered trademark. Although the content of PDQ documents can be used freely as text, it cannot be identified as an NCI PDQ cancer information summary unless it is presented in its entirety and is regularly updated. However, an author would be permitted to write a sentence such as &#8220;NCI&#8217;s PDQ cancer information summary about breast cancer prevention states the risks succinctly: [include excerpt from the summary].&#8221;</p>

6001<p id="_AboutThis_14" tabindex="-1">

6002The preferred citation for this PDQ summary is:</p>

6003<p id="_AboutThis_15" tabindex="-1">

6004PDQ&#174; Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board. PDQ Childhood Vascular Tumors Treatment. Bethesda, MD: National Cancer Institute. Updated &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. Available at: <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq">

6005https://www.cancer.gov/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/child-vascular-tumors-treatment-pdq</a>

6006. Accessed &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. [PMID: 26844334]</p>

6007<p id="_AboutThis_16" tabindex="-1">

6008Images in this summary are used with permission of the author(s), artist, and/or publisher for use within the PDQ summaries only. Permission to use images outside the context of PDQ information must be obtained from the owner(s) and cannot be granted by the National Cancer Institute. Information about using the illustrations in this summary, along with many other cancer-related images, is available in <a href="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/" title="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/">

6009Visuals Online</a>

6010, a collection of over 2,000 scientific images.

6014<section id="_AboutThis_17">

6015<h3 id="_AboutThis_17_toc">

6016Disclaimer</h3>

6017<p id="_AboutThis_18" tabindex="-1">

6018Based on the strength of the available evidence, treatment options may be described as either &#8220;standard&#8221; or &#8220;under clinical evaluation.&#8221; These classifications should not be used as a basis for insurance reimbursement determinations. More information on insurance coverage is available on Cancer.gov on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care">

6019Managing Cancer Care</a>

6020 page.</p>
